GAME: Hansa Teutonica

RATING: 8.5
The first euro game I ever played and one of the first I played again upon my recent reintroduction to board gaming. Unlike most euros, planning won't win the game. Reprioritizing on the fly and knowing when to put all emphasis on a single element are skills more valuable than formulaic anticipation. It truly is the stock market- Texas Hold 'Em style. A beloved game that I rated low for reasons I don't understand. Upgraded from 7.1 to 8.5.

RATING: 8

I'm a bit down on pure eurogames - like this one - at the moment but this is an exception beccuase it has lots of options, very good player interaction and plays really quickly for a game with so many decision points. Highly recommended and the best eurogame for several years at the time it was published

RATING: N/A
First game impressions:

Feels like an old-school euro (read: El Grande, Ginkgopolis), not the new age stuff (AquaSphere or Fields of Arle) with little interaction and lots of chrome. Also feels like an old-school euro in that the theme is not only paste-on, but its counter productive. It's ultimately an abstract with a fairly simple ruleset that allows you to get in each others way. Downside is there is an efficiency of sorts in terms of building your player board that helps dictate regional play and hinders more emergent games (the game plays over 2 arcs; efficiency gains on the north/south regions, then pivot to city grabbing). Neat little tactical game, and does well if folks don't over think it. Not sure I'd buy it, but maybe; not many euros occupy this space.

RATING: 9.5
Totally devoid of theme, drab artwork, convoluted ruleset, and yet this is a great game.  It scales from 2-5 players, it offers a myriad of possible strategies, and other than the introduction of bonus tiles, there is no luck.  I have almost no idea how to win this game, and yet I keep wanting to come back for more.  The goal of course is to score the most victory points and this can be accomplished in several ways: 1) bonus tiles, 2) placement of offices, 3) scoring of offices when trading routes are closed, 4) end scoring of controlled offices, 5) longest network of offices at the end of the game, 6) completed abilities, 7) network connection between the two end cities (forget their names), and 8) points scored from closing in the lefthand corner (forget the cityname).

The game also has several end conditions, the manipulation of these I think are one of the keys to winning the game: 1) 20 points scored during gameplay, 2) 10 completed cities, and 3) running out of bonus tiles.  On your turn, you have a choice between several different actions: 1) collect more cubes, needed for valuable placements, 2) move cubes on the board (a very important ability), 3) establish a trading route, 4) placing cubes/circles (traders or merchants),and 5) displacing traders or merchants (a confrontational aspect to the game).  

This is a very deep game and yet it feels easy and "light" to play, gameplay is relatively quick.  It doesn't look original, just the opposite, but in actuality it is quite a unique game.  Don't overlook this one like I almost did. 

Own the [boardgame=83404]East[/boardgame] and [boardgame=162179]Britannia[/boardgame] expansions and the [boardgame=213292]Emperor's Favour[/boardgame] promo.

RATING: 6
1 play / 3 players

LIKE: Unique bumping mechanisms make this game, and create interesting interactions among players. Good use of an asymmetrical board, where one area is highly contested over (the increasing actions upgrade), then the rest of the board is built out from there.

DISLIKE: Very dry game, almost themeless. Difficult to find a strategy for first time beginners. The tiles add a luck component that can be quite powerful.

OVERALL: It's good, but difficult to get to the table.

RATING: 8.5
Solid game. It is comparable to Endeavor - and it probably is better for all that. Certainly the strategy is more convoluted and much more confrontational. Lots of take-that in this one. My only take away is there is seemingly a very important early game in the extra action section - and if one also controls the neighboring cities, that player is in for a leading scoring position. Makes the early game a bit too obvious and requires opponents to play against them. In any event, I really like this one. Subsequent games are making me like it even more. When you get a table of nearly equal experience, this game is very, very good indeed.

RATING: 6.5
Reasonably amusing, but the map is much too large. It should have been constructed of tiles and expanded as the players needed more space, to keep people together and in active conflict. As it was, in my one play, we had only one town on the whole map with two competing cubes, and we each had a small stack of special action chips allowing us to swap the order of town control cubes - rendering it a pointless exercise to compete over.

I am fairly sure that this is partly due to us playing poorly, but with a tighter map we would have been forced into playing properly and we would have had a better time of it.

The game ended just as we were starting to accomplish things and get to a position where we could start to compete for territory. But, if it hadn't stopped there, probably there would have been a risk of experienced players being able to max out most of their upgrades.

So - decent game, but poorly balanced to create the best gaming experience. Unless you're all experienced players of Hansa Teutonica, the quality of play will degrade.

RATING: 10
Many plays: This is about as perfect as euros get. There is SO much interaction, SO many different paths to victory, and every play will wiggle itself out differently. Yes, sitting to the left of a bad player will give you an advantage, but its not as severe as in something like Puerto Rico. But games with a high degree of interaction demand things of you. If you get blown out in a learning game, play it again. And again. And you'll see, that DAMN this game is fantastic. Even when I (always) lose I love it. Without question never leaving my collection, and lowering the rating of many games in my collection just because its so good.

+ 2016 advent promo
+ brittannia & east maps

Dry as hell. Yes.
Fun as hell? Absolutely.

This is a game you can punch and teach in about 5 minutes. Takes a few turns to see how it works. Moving cubes around is very unintuitive and it turns the area control on its head. Bumping is also not something you want to do unless you stand to benefit more than your opponent, and learning when this is takes experience. Unique game in many ways that feels incredibly old school, tightly tuned, and very far from the hottness. Couldn't stop thinking about it after my first play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
2-5(5)

Preliminary rating:  This seems like the real deal.  I'm a mechanics geek and so I love the technology tracks and how they tangibly advance what you can do in the game (as opposed to just boosting you on some VP scale, or open up abilities that are, functionally, not going to affect the game dramatically).

The fact that so many points are scored at the end adds a good amount of mystery and tension to the game, and makes the initial 20 point VP track more a race than the game itself.  The way traders and merchants keep going to "stock" (the unusable pile) is a good dynamic.  The blocking is handled well, the tracks seem well balanced, the decisions about how to use merchants interesting.  And etc.  It played fast, and was short (unlike Loyang, good God), and was fun.

Obviously, the box, theme, and artwork are a bit bland (I think the board looks nice, but route-building in Old Germany???), and the play is still so far abstracted from the theme (could be a problem with the girlfriend), but when I enjoyed it this much, I don't mind.

Need to play with more because the interactions would just be that much greater.  Also, need to play with the 2p "pawn"; we've played without, as the province thing seemed to complicate things, though I can now understand why it's in the rules: to encourage blocking and the like.

RATING: 7
An austere Euro whose Hanseatic trading theme is so thin it could as easily have been about the spread of viruses in the human body.

Happily, a good game lies underneath the paper thin theme and the uninspiring artwork.  At heart, the game is classic Euro fare: develop an engine that allows you to do more things more efficiently and keep that in balance with doing other things that score you victory points.  Where Hansa scores with me is by adding a hefty amount of player interaction.  This isn't just about beating opponents to actions they also wish to take, it's   exploiting their actions for your own advantage and making the things they need to do more costly.

The game also feels quite dynamic, with quick player turns in which pieces are placed, moved and taken off the board.  While I may be thwarted this turn, there's every possibility that by next turn my way will be clear.
A good game, with multiple strategic possibilities and yet somehow it doesn't excite me.

Production quality is decent.  The artwork is dull but functional.  The rules suffered somewhat in translation to English.

RATING: 7
This game is the ephemeral pop song around here.  The first day that I played this, I played it 4 times because nobody wanted to play anything else.  Time does seem to pass very quickly when playing.

I like the fact that there is a gradual learning curve which slowly unfolds as you get more experience with the game.  However I can't shake the feeling that the map is unbalanced in ways that will reveal themselves over time.  I think that the action track on the bottom as well as the city Stade on the top which allows building privileges can be locked up and allow people to run away with the game.  As a result of this you should play with others of approximately the same experience level or suffer from Puerto Rico syndrome.  

Blocking others is a mandatory part of the game, so if you have folks that run away this aspect of gaming, this is not for them.

Not going to recommend for 3, because like many games it is to easy for 2 players to focus on each other, and let the third person walk away with the game.  Likewise, one friend cobbled together pieces for a sixth player using the color black, and I think that the game is a little too tight with 6.  Plays best with 4-5.

RATING: 8.5
Only played it three times, the rating will likely change with more plays...

The good: 
- Very low luck factor (other than other player's plays)
- Lots of approaches and avenues to victory
- Not too complicated and seems pretty easy to pickup (even if describing the came can seem overwhelming)
- While there is interaction and counter moves (in general) it's light on "fuck you over" moves. The choices are more about whether you want to take the penalty to mess with other players

The bad:
- Explanation can be lengthy and overwhelming
- Kinda hard to gauge how you're doing during the game (for some this is a good thing)
- I'm not entirely convinced it's balanced, extra actions seem much more powerful compared to the other abilities (especially early game).

More to come I'm sure!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Hansa Teutonica basically has nothing like it. It's route connection... but it's not like a route connection game. It's worker placement... but no other worker placement game is like it. Heck, it's even an RPG!

This game has a quite open decision space, but it directs it - the skills and bonus chips allow you to easily read what moves are viable, and then it's a matter of figuring out how to value those options. Also, how cool is it that there's a game where a strong strategy is "get in everyone's way"?

RATING: 9
The games go beyond simply route-building for VP in so many ways. It's a game with aggression... but one where you want to be the recipient of that aggression. It's a game with an engine-building aspect that creates interesting decisions but also a foundation for other players to "block" you. But most impressive, there seems to be numerous strategies to work from. Very good game.

[UPDATE] After playing this game 10 times, I'm moving my rating from a 7 to a 9. Why? Because this game is unique, interactive, and fluid. It's a game where every decision matters and one that develops quite differently depending on the players' actions. And furthermore the game creates a lot of depth/replayability for a light rulset. If there was any strong criticism against the game, it would be that novice/inexperience players can upset the balance of the game. But with a group of equally skilled players, the game is just marvelous to play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I appreciate how Hansa Teutonica seems to have some depth, but comes with such few components (a main board, a few small player boards and bunch of cubes and few disks to round it out) and play quicker than you would think it would (the game I played was with two new people including me, and I think it).

Having said that, I got two problems with this game. First, the game is a route connection game combined with an engine building game. You build your engine by removing cubes from your board after completing routes in the corners of the map. So for the first half of the game, everybody was just hanging around those parts, just trying to complete or block somebody's completion of those route. Call me crazy, but I feel like a route building game should focus more on actually building the route

That feeds into my problem with the game. Engine building is already a pretty newbie unfriendly mechanic (you have to know the quickest way of building the engine), and allowing players to block other players from getting the engine upgrades just allows more experienced players to run with the game.

I don't know, these seems to be beginner's problems. I obviously don't have problem with engine building, abstract-ish euros. I even found the map was okay. While I'm not sure that I really like it, I'm not adverse at all to trying this game again.

RATING: 9
Szuper! Többen azzal riogattak, hogy mennyire száraz játék. Nem tudom, ezt pontosan hogy kell érteni. Tény, hogy nem tematikus, hanem inkább egy absztaktabb, eurogémereknek való darab, de nekem nagy élményt nyújtott (talán mert én is az vagyok?). Az előrejelzések, és a szabálymagyarázat alapján valami ijesztőbbre számítottam, de ez egy kifejezetten könnyed, pörgős játék, csak meg kell szokni a világát, mert elég egyedi! Gyorsan sorra kerülünk (3 fős parti volt), és általában a jó és a még jobb közül lehet választani, nehézségét és ütemét tekintve a rondellásokhoz tudom hasonlítani (Hamburgum), csak itt még nagyobb a szabadság, mert nem köt minket a rondella. És azt hiszem ezzel el is érkeztem a játék lényegéhez: a néhány jól kitalált akció szinte végtelen lehetőséget kínál, és számtalan nyerő stratégia sejlett fel a két parti során. Kreatív emberek előnyben! A játék folyamatosan alkalmazkodást igényel a többiekhez, az is bizonytalan, hogy mikor fejeződik be a játék, szóval erősen taktikai játék! A segédlet jó, a játék teljesen nyelvfüggetlen (bár magyar szabály még nincs), és szerintem kifejezetten szép! Aki tud lelkesedni pusztán egy jól kitalált mechanizmusért, annak kötelező darab. 
Ami engem zavart, az a túl gyors befejezés, a 20 pont elérése elég váratlanul véget vethet a játéknak...
---
5-en is szuper!
---
Ketten sajnos nem olyan átütő, bár a plusz szabályok nélkül nem rossz úgy se, talán kicsit nagy a pálya. A plusz szabályok viszont eléggé megváltoztatják, megtörténhet, hogy végig csak a tábla egy szegletében zajlik a játék, és a fejlesztések nagy részéhez nem nyúlunk. Ez is érdekes, de teljesen más játék.

RATING: 4
I disliked how fast it ended.  Most of the features were not used during my first session.  The actions that I took felt very repetitive and samey.  Perhaps I should have tried out some other actions or area(s) of the board.  However, the game encourages players to compete in the same areas, such as the extra actions area.    

Update: I played a 2nd session with a different group.  The end didn't trigger so fast this time around, so it was nice to see more development, such as more areas of the board in use, more tech upgrades, larger networks, bonus tiles. etc.  However, the actions still felt very repetitive and samey, which isn't good considering that I tried new approaches, different tech upgrades, different areas of the board, etc.

I find Hansa T really, really dry.  I also feel that there is a lack of control when playing with 4 players.  Probably my biggest issue with the game is the blocking of routes.  Although blocking provides player interaction and competition for choice locations, it just isn't very fun.  I hate having to decide between blocking Player A, Player B, or Player C.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
:d10-3::d10-4:
Hansa Teutonica rises above the Euro haze as one of the pinnacles of modern Euro design.

Played as an efficiency optimization game, it wouldn't be worth bothering with, as just a slightly more variable version of Endeavor. But played as a cutthroat game of shifting incentives, with every move an offering or a coercion, the game is sublime.

There is almost literally no other traditional euro I would rather play. And that really just depends upon how broad your definition is of 'traditional euro'.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[i]Posted: 2/8/11, Rated: 7[/i]
{2P: ?, 3P: ?, 4P: 7, 5P: ?}

[i]Hansa Teutonica[/i] was the [i][thing=18602]Caylus[/thing][/i] of 2009.  What I mean by that analogy is that it received a ton of buzz.  The world was going to alter its orbit for [i]Hansa Teutonica[/i] as it had for other supreme buzz-generators.  My challenge is that I haven't been impressed with [i][thing=18602]Caylus[/thing][/i].  I know how to play, and I've enjoyed it on [url=http://www.brettspielwelt.de/Spiele/Caylus/]BrettspielWelt[/url], but my face-to-face games have been blasé.  How would another buzz-generator fare?  The answer is as nuanced as the game itself.

Components-wise, this game is a throwback to the Eurogames of yesteryear.  There are a few cardboard tokens, but for the most part there are a lot of wooden cubes and discs.  This isn't a bad thing at all, but the spartan nature of these components made me realize how I'd gotten away from games with wooden cubes over the past couple of years!

RATING: 8
First Play: I'd read the rules for this on the way to Essen and decided that there was no game there and celined to buy. Then there was such a buzz I thought I should reread them again, still nothing. When a close friend said how good it was I decided to chip and buy it. Good job I did.

Hansa is a great example of needing to play a game to understand it. The mechanisms are simple but the strategies are incredibly deep. The possibilities to block and thwart are wonderful and the wide spread of point scoring oportunities keep the game open from start to finish. End game points were close suggesting this has been well play tested. 

Subsequent play confirms this has incredible depth. An immediate favourite.

RATING: 8
Worker placement Euro with a lot of depth.  I've played it about a half-dozen times and I'm STILL not sure of the best strategy.  (Well, other than "Get more actions!!!", but that's just Worker Placement 101.)  An interesting twist on the genre, you have to place workers in groups for them to do anything useful.  Definitely gets more fun once players start embracing "griefing" tactics that force opponents to either change plans or give you a cookie.

Be warned, the rulebook is ass; the game desperately needs player handouts with scoring summaries and descriptions of the hieroglyphics on the collectable tokens.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
OK, as a game design, this is almost ridiculous.  A hundred different possible moves, five things you need to build in your engine, many ways to score victory points (and having to choose between scoring victory points and building your engine).  It is pretty much impossible to come up with a plan, there are far too many variables and choices to make.  Caveat AP-or.

After two plays I can see why hobbyists like the game, even if it isn't for me.  There are many options but experimentation will quickly whittle down the options to a handful.  It's pretty much a sandbox game in that the player is given freedom to explore, try out many many different alternative paths to victory.

I still propose that as a game design there are far, far too many things going on here.  The game is short and, dare I use the word, elegant, but it could use more focus.  There are whole chunks that (in my opinion) add YATTTA (Yet Another Thing To Think About) that the game doesn't really benefit from (and I wouldn't say are "fun").

For example, the special action tiles (shaped like plates of food), for me, are "a bridge too far".  I think the design would be improved with their complete omission, and when I play I have no choice but to ignore them, because I have too much other stuff to consider.  Of course, my opponents aren't ignoring them, then all my planning becomes moot because they can one-time break the rules and scupper my plans.  Ugh.  OK, blame me and not the game, if you must.

I like the idea of the game, and it all works together well.  I guess I'm looking for Hansa Teutonica 2, a game that has the same basic idea and mechanisms but a player can jump into their first game and enjoy and not feel overwhelmed.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Who needs a good theme when you have mechanics like this? Love action point allowance, love upgrading, love multiple game ending conditions, love little wooden cubes. With all that emotion aside the thing that really makes this game is how you are wanting people to take your spot over. The player getting attacked ending more often then not in a better position then the attacker solves so many problems I have with many area control games. It keeps the player interaction but stops the gang up on the leader aspect as well as reduces the chaos. Every game I’ve played has been very different. Brilliant stuff.

RATING: 6
Underwhelmed.  A set collection and route-building game that could have been good.  It's too chaotic (and too easy to lose your positions in the cities) and the special action discs are just too powerful.  The fact that you can pick up a disc as well as claim something on the route that the disc is sitting on is a big deal.  Then add to this the fact that the disc is basically a free action that substantially breaks the rules and that you get bonus points at the end of the game for both USED and unused discs... jeez... this game should be called Discs of Hansa.  Perhaps there is something wrong with the English translation, the extreme power of the discs seems to break the game.  It just feels like you're spinning your wheels and eventually someone will win... probably by obtaining more discs or better discs than you.

RATING: 9
Although I play horribly in this game, it is one of my favorites!  Even though EVERYONE will be going for additional actions early in the game, you MUST get in that competition.  There is no way to keep up in board position without maintaining equal footing on the number of actions you are taking.  This game does seem to benefit the opponent of a displacement-happy player, but I think that is intentional and ensures that all players realize the benefit for being displaced.  I love the hard decision about taking a politician in a city for early game VP generation, or taking the action board advancement that helps your economy.  Wow, I knew this would be a great game and it definitely didn't disappoint -- even though my play is disappointing!  I wonder if the game would be slightly better if players didn't take ALL of their actions in one shot, but had to intermix those with other players?  Or, at least, if the player order didn't always have to go by table seating position?

RATING: 8.5
What a surprise! 

It's kind of an old game, with a dry cover and a board that is boring as hell... but the game itself is brilliant!

Intuitive gameplay, quick turns, a lot of different strategies... and the most intriguing part of it is the interaction between players. Your move will affect other players moves, you can play in "friendly" mode and you still have to look out for what your opponent is doing and sooner or later you'll have to do something, or in "jerk" mode... and that way is so much fun! This way, your focus is to block each others moves, the game takes longer, but the challenge is much more attractive.

I love the game. Theme wise is zero, but who cares when the gameplay is so interesting?! Another plus to the game. It doesn't matter the player number count, the game runs smoothly around 60min.

Check it out!

RATING: 8.5
Juego abstracto donde los haya. El tablero es una red de ciudades en las que los jugadores pueden ir estableciendo oficinas y rellenando rutas que conecten las ciudades entre sí. Cada jugador tiene un tablero propio que le permitirá mejorar ciertas acciones. Al final de la partida hay una puntuación por la ruta más larga, por el estado de tu tablero de acciones, etc. Es muy cerebral. Las reglas son sencillas y se juega rápido, pero tiene una profundidad bastante considerable. Interactivo a más no poder. Muy recomendable.

Lo compre en junio 2013 en juegame store, con el vale de las CLBSK13. Me salió por 8 euros :)

Vendido en la bsk a fermanowar en octubre 2014 por 21€

RATING: 5
One play. A game that features familiar mechanics, in a different way. Feels like a mixture of Endeavor, China, and several other games that includes the general/personal supply. Not that this is a negative, they are familiar. Quite competitive, with much opportunity to dislodge other players' positions, with a lot of jostling for positions with players' cubes on trade routes between cities. A key feature is using these placements to one's advantage to slow your opponent and gain position when he tries to dislodge you. I feel this mechanic is somewhat of a negative in that a player can get a nice position when an opponent forces them off a road. Although this is a strategic placement by the player, it almost feels kingmaking when an opponent ignores the advantage he is giving to the player being moved. Scoring is somewhat involved, which increases the weight for the game.

RATING: 9
2-5 | (3,5) | 60 min | 12+ | Andreas Steding
This game seems to polarize players: they either love it or hate it. In my case, a lukewarm first impression (beginners are always a bit lost in the first play) has changed to the point where HT is now one of my favorite games in my collection (and possibly best of 2009). I love how each game is different based on the players' decisions. You can use a strategy to win one game, use the same strategy in a second game and fail miserably.
---------------------------------------------
(March 2011) I still consider HT a 9 with 4 or 5 experienced players. However, rating drops to an 8 for 3-player games, which I find less satisfying. The game also suffers when one of the players is a newbie or lets the player to his left get away with his moves (à la Puerto Rico).

RATING: 8
I really like the idea behind this game, and thought it was pretty fun.  You really do need to be careful about one player running away with more actions.   I liked the tradeoff between building your power through moving your pegs up and controlling the board.

The one complaint in our 3 player game was that it seemed that the incremental points you gain through ownership of adjacent towns really seemed to be a tiny drop in the bucket compared to the big points that are awarded later.   Perhaps that is a problem with the 3 player game though.  Would like to try 4.

Edit to add:  much different experience with 5 players.  Amazingly, there wasn't a whole lot of battles over territory, as there are still a ton of different things that can be achieved on a turn.   It seems to me like the board might be a little too open -- a little tighter board for 5 players would probably make for a better game.

Add Nov 2010:   After a few more plays it seems that there truly are a bunch of different paths to victory in this one.   There are certainly some effective ways to win, but these ways also have counters which stop a player from running away with a victory easily.

RATING: 9
Updated 12/4/10.

What can I say about Hansa Teutonica that hasn't been said already, and better, by others?  It's a beautiful throwback game of cube movement and network building.  Although initially spartan-seeming, the game is quintessentially elegant.  The rules are easy to understand, but the depth is surprising.  There are so many possible approaches to the game (although new players tend to overvalue extra actions), that each game unfolds as its own story.  This is what multi-player chess would feel like if I remotely liked chess.

If there is a notable downside to the game, it's that new players can fail to recognize the significance of their actions and inadvertently and unwisely benefit a particular player.  Fortunately, the ramifications of new player mistakes are not tied to player seating order, so this is less of an issue than in, say, a hyper-competitive game of Puerto Rico.  

The other things keeping the game from a higher rating are trivial.  The game is very abstract, so I lack the enjoyment of thematic immersion that I find in most great games.  The fluidity of the game undermines a bit of tension for me: so many viable options exist that I don't feel the consequences of my mistakes weighing on me like I do in, say, Tigris & Euphrates.  Finally, the two-player rules are simply less engaging than with higher player counts. 

Still, a solid all-around game that I always enjoy playing and would recommend to nearly anyone interested in pure Eurogames.

RATING: 8
The epitome of the traditional Euro game: Dry theme, uninviting cover art, and drab board. But don't let it's appearance fool you, this is a rock-solid strategy game! Players must build trade routes between various cities to unlock bonuses and establish area control to gain victory points. I love games that incorporate technology trees so I really love being able to customize my engine but upgrading certain parts as they fit into my overall strategy. I also loved the choice of short-term versus long-term rewards when completing routes. The game definitely forces you to play a bit mean by preventing other players from completing routes but it's not enough to ruin plans and cause frustration. This is a prime example of why you shouldn't judge of book by its cover. Excellent game that I'm already looking forward to try again.

RATING: 7
Based on 1 play with 4 players:

Pretty impressed with this game, actually! It doesn't look like much but there's a lot to like. The rules are actually pretty simple (once you get past the obtuse rulebook) but there's a lot of different paths to pursue and just the right amount of fun group-think to guide your (re)actions.

Ontop of that the pacing is great. Turns move fast and there's some light opportunistic player interaction without resorting to just dull blocking or cruelty (which... I love mean games, but it's nice to have a variety!)

I think this could pretty easily be a great "next step" gateway game.

RATING: 9.5
This is one of my favorite games! You need to make a lot of tactical moves, but you kind of need a strategy too, and I like this mix. I like that there isn't any overpowered strategy and that you often need to do a little bit of everything in order to succeed, but you can also go heavy in one direction if you want. Sure, there is the extra action route which is pretty important to get early on, but I actually played a 3 player game in which I had 2 actions when the other players had 5... And I still didn't end up last. And I've played a lot of games where I've had 3 actions and another player had 5, and I still won. So this is something I really appreciate, as it's unlike many other games where more actions is crucial in order to win. 

There are lots of hard decisions to make, especially when you want to score a route and you have to decide if you want the bonus power or an office. I also really like how it's an aggressive interactive eurogame because you screw the other players over by placing cubes on their routes and they then need to displace you etc. - this is a huge part of the game, and even though you cannot block anything "for real" as in a normal worker placement game, it's still of huge importance to block the routes other people are building! The frequency and how aggressive the blocking is, varies from game to game; I played one 3p game in which it took almost 30 minutes for anybody to get his 3rd action - such a weird game.
Also, blocking happens of course even more in 5p games, but I actually don't love it at that player count, unlike many others. I really love it with 3p and 4p, but even 2p with my wife is AWESOME!

It usually takes less than an hour with 3p, and I've played games with my wife that took about 20-25 minutes if any of us rushed the ending. "Rushed the ending", you say? Yes, by choosing a strategy in which you build offices in either 1p value cities or between cities that are often claimed, meaning that you'll score points every time the route is claimed (and you can even try to get the 7 instant points by connecting the two red cities). By doing so, you can score a lot of points and reach 20p before the strategies of the other players start to take off; because the game ends instantly once any player has 20 points. But it's a risky strategy for sure, and longterm strategies, when the game ends either because the plate bonus disks run out of 10 cities are full, can often result in blowout wins; I've seen winning scores in the 20's and in the 90's, and this is awesome! In other games you always know that the winning score will be e.g. 40 points +/- 5 points, but in HT you can sort of manipulate how the game is going to end and which strategies will succeed. At least when you've played it 50+ times which I have - and I *still* learn something new in every game I play! In some games the "longest network" scoring can give you 30 points, in other 3. In some games you can get 3 disks in Coellen, in other games you get none. So. Many. Possibilities.

There is so many other things to touch upon as to why this game is awesome... Which abilities to develop, the fight for establishing offices, the fight for the bonus plates, the 5 different ways to score points (fully developed abilities, number of plates, Coellen table, controlled offices, longest route) is truly great, and you can really specify and concentrate on certain paths during the game, and none of them are overpowered. It's a wonderfully balanced game full of options right from the beginning, and no two games play out the same as you can pursue vastly different strategies each time. The player count also has an impact on how the game is played, and while many like it with 4-5, I prefer 2 and 3 (with higher player count, the first expansion map is really great, and with lower the second is pretty good as well, but overall I actually prefer the base map).

A truly excellent game! One of my alltime favorite games - luckily in both my own and my wife's top 5!

RATING: 8
An excellent game, and definitely one that I am very pleased to own.  A "route" game with some great "screw you" possibilities, and definitely one that should hit the table regularly.  Easy to play and learn, but it will take a while to learn the strategies of play.

There is a seriously broken rule about the Librae Sophia - the rules state that you can, as ONE ACTION, move as many of your merchants/traders as your Librae Sophia number.  This is broken, broken, broken!  The LS number should represent the maximum number of "Move" actions you can take on your turn, not the number of dudes you can move for ONE ACTION.  It should take TWO ACTIONS to "move" TWO merchants/traders.  DO NOT PLAY the way the rules are written, as it is completely broken and ruins the ability of players to block each other on the roads.  This needs to be fixed and addressed!

Otherwise, an excellent game!

EDIT:  Bumped this rating to a 10.  I always want to play this game, and I never expect that to change.  Easily my favorite game of 2009.

EDIT:  Back down to a "8".  I have a lot of games to play, and I have recently changed many of my ratings to reflect this.  "10" is a tough nut to crack, and I think that I have too many games that I "always want to play".  So, due to lack of continually playing, I just don't see this as being a "10" for me any more.  But I do like the game, and probably won't turn it down if someone suggested a play.

RATING: 9
Well outside of possibly El Grande, I can't think of a more stereotypical "cube pushing Euro game". What can I say - the game look boring, the theme is dull, and it has no flashy components. Yet, I love this game. This is a true example of brilliant game play making all the difference.

I've played this a few times now and every single time someone was convinced they found the one and only way to play, and ironically every single time that player lost. There are a lot of variations here and I'm still discovering new avenues of play. I've very much resisted reading the "some strategy tips" at the back of the manual since I think half the fun is developing these strategies for myself.

The downside is the theme does turn some people away, and it does take a few plays before someone can get used to it. When I get the right players together though, this game shines.

RATING: 4
Initial rating an "8." OK, so the theme is pretty thin and the game has an abstract feel, but it's a challenge and it's engaging. Lots of options, but too few choices to take advantage of all of them. We'll be playing this a number of times in the near future, and I'm looking forward to getting to know this game better.

2nd play, and I'm dropping this to a 7. I got crushed in this game, so I'm hoping this is not "sour grapes." Two things that struck me this time were the utter lack of a theme, and the fact that there's some strictly better choices in the opening. Everyone wants actions and the buildings adjacent to it, and I'm not sure how you win without grabbing at least a 3rd action early on. Once you get past that opening though, the game really starts to offer a wealth of choices. It's a good game, one I'll happily play for its mechanics, but I'll let others suggest it.

Update: Ugh. 3rd play, and I'm dropping this to a 5. I must be missing the "hansa" chromosome, as this game and I just aren't clicking. I've got the same criticisms as mentioned above... or maybe I'm just missing something. I'll probably end up playing this again because it's a favorite in my gaming circles, but the problem might be I'm playing against folks that play this monthly, while I'm playing this every 6 months or later and getting pummeled.

RATING: 8.5
Over the years, my interest level in true Euro designs has waned a bit. It takes something a bit special to grab my attention. I can't remember the last time a Euro design impressed me like this one. 

The game is very abstract (but who cares).  Its got nice production values and I like the board.

Biggest negative is that there can be a turn-order issue if you follow a naive player. There is a bit of a Puerto Rico quality to the game, where you can tee up excellent opportunities for the person after you if you don't pay attention. I've seen more than one game go off-kilter from one person's questionable play. On the other hand, if you play with a full table of experienced players its pretty brutal and fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I flat out don't understand what all the fuss is about. HT is a game that I never would have tried if so many people hadn't been praising it as it looked like a dry, generic Euro to me. On playing it, the random first impression I had from watching it being played seems about spot on.

HT is a game that throws several Euro elements in a blender. You have limited actions, the court/provinces mechanic from El Grande, and the connections value from about 900 other Euro titles. You place cubes on the board during many of your actions, sometimes for direct benefit, sometimes just to block other people as they will then remove the cube which gives you even more resources to work with. There are a lot of subtleties that I won't go into here, but that is the basic concept of the game. You have special powers fueled by completing connections that other players can interfere with for their own benefit. Completing connections can either score points directly or improve powers like having the ability to place more pieces or scoring more for end-game networks.

Though I can see the room for strategic and tactical depth, I frankly don't understand the enthusiasm this has generated. I was playing this with one of the most fun, easy-going groups around, and I still found it dry as dirt and very mechanical. There is no spark or special twist here that makes me want to ask for this game again, nothing that makes it stand out. To me, it is the stereotypical Euro game, everything fits together tightly, but there doesn't seem to be any soul or hook to really differentiate it.

RATING: 8
Hansa Teutonica has been on and off my new game radar as a faint blip ever since it appeared in 2009. I remember that reading through a brief German description on some Polish website (talk about language confusion...) left me with the impression of a complex game with some elements which usually ring a few warning bells with me. I put it on the to try-list as a ‘possible’, but regrettably could not find a free spot at the tables of Argentum Spieleverlag to give it a go. Fast forward a year, when Dutch publisher 999 Games announces this title in a Dutch edition and me trying it during a minor games fair.

Hansa Teutonica has a very interesting learning curve for first timers. First you see them struggle with all the rules, and about halfway through you see that the game's mechanic begins to ‘click’, and then they turn into a bunch of homicidal maniacs who show absolutely no mercy whatsoever to whomever is doing the explaining. I was no exception, by the way. And after the first game is completed they either love or hate it. The hate (dislike rather) originates from the game's very dry subject matter. It attempts to portray the hustle and bustle of various cities and towns in the Hanseatic League, but omits the usual elements of trade, making money and shipping goods. Instead the game is far more a logistical exercise where focus is on manipulating the properties of a (mathematical) graph, and this is what bothers the dislikers. If people like the game, they invariably report that despite the first rough half, they already see that there are quite a lot of ways to play this game, and that they will do better next time.

Usually I am not very fond of the growth mechanics employed by the author to drive the game, but Hansa Teutonica is an exception. The reason is that the actions are exceptionally clear and distinct, and come with obvious cause-effect relationships. Doing more actions is obviously good, as is enticing others to kick you out of trade routes (as you get more cubes on the board as a consequence). Getting more cubes in your supply is also good. The game therefore transforms into an ensemble where players are requested to become creative with a lot of small basic building blocks, and that is about the juiciest carrot you can dangle in front of my face in order to get me to play a game. I've played but two games to date, and give the game a conservative rating as a consequence, but if the feeling of short gameplay coupled with creative thinking persists, I can only envisage the rate going up.

[b]Update[/b] The rate is going to stay put, I'm afraid. The inevitable happened: players began to learn to work the action track, and this is such a strong track that all responses to it become scripted. You do not need all 5 actions to be competitive: I've ended up quite close to the winners using only 3 actions to realise this. But Hansa Teutonica always seems to end up in 'actions' and 'responses to actions' kind-of play, with a tug of war gouverning the timing of when the game ends. It's not that the game is bad: but it simply doesn't appear to work in a way I like things to work.

[b]Update 2[/b] That all said, once the game works as it should, it is like thermite and ice (see [url=https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=w6cMmk8LZgQ]here[/url]), and certainly worthy of an 8. I'm a little conflicted here to award the game this rate, because it presupposes people who [i]really[/i] know what they are doing, and [i]really[/i] take the proper steps to reign in fast movers, or force feed them into lethargy (out of fear of ending the game before they acquire a sufficient number of points). And I don't really like the fact that a big part of the game is simply going to 3 actions, and then copperatively making it very expensive for everyone to get more: this aspect is really fairly scripted. Deviate from it at your own peril. Ack, what the hell, 8 it is. Good design, Andreas. Here's to hoping that new expansion makes the game worth playing at 2 too!

RATING: 8.75
This is a game that is much more fun than it has a right to be.  The rules are complex, the theme is barely there, and it seems like it's just another Eurogame involving route building and resource management.  Except it's great!

The agony of choices is potent - a real challenge that might drive some people crazy, but is a trait I really love in a game.  Each choice is often predicated on selecting another action or route first - how far ahead can you plan?  And can you roll with the changes around you?  There is certainly no clear path to victory, and it's very hard to tell who's ahead until you count up points at the end.

My minor complaints are that the game is rather dry and with AP-prone players it drags on longer than it should.  But this won't stop me from bringing it to the table again soon.

(Small note - the Z-Man edition has a misprint in the rules when explaining bonus markers and what they do.)

RATING: 7
This may be my new canonical example of a Eurogame: lots of little cubes, bland economic theme over a positional game of networks and graphs, a variety of approaches to scoring points. Doesn't mean I don't like it - quite the contrary - but oi, does it intimidate some players.

Most games that reward/encourage minor and arbitrary interference with other players's plans, I would call  frustrating. Somehow, HT instead simply turns this facet into a lesson: don't telegraph what you're doing unless you're prepared to displace a blocking player. Perhaps it's because the penalty for being blocked is relatively light - only 1/3 or 2/3 of an action even at starting payment tech - and doesn't impinge upon one's immediate tactical goals unless one's supply is riding the brink of being empty.

Nearly every game I've played - some short, some long - has been won by a player with either the most or second-most offices; the one exception was won by someone who put merchants on 3 of the 4 bonus-scoring opportunities. Playing around with tech is fun, but if you don't ultimately turn that into board position, those 4 points/track are not going to salvage your score.

RATING: 8
My first couple plays of this were "meh". I really had trouble with the displacement rules, and they didn't seem to fit with the Euro style I was used to. Why would I pay twice as much to displace an opponent who then benefits? It seemed to me that players caught up in a competition for spaces would be at a stalemate while the remaining player(s) ran away with the game. 

I have now come to appreciate this additional aspect of the game, and my fears are unjustified. That is the beauty of the Liber Sophia (transfer action) for the displacing player or the player who keeps getting blocked. Just pick up your pieces and go to a different route (all for only one action). 

I really like games where there are mutliple ways to score VPs. This game has many paths to victory, and not near enough time to do everything you'll want to do. Watch out for your opponents though, because if you don't work to bring down the leader (esp. if he/she has 5 actions/turn) then he/she will win handily. 

This game almost seems to end too early. I like it so much,  often want to play it back to back, because it's so short.

RATING: 9
This is a terrific game, and is pretty much all I could want in a game.

There are multiple paths to victory, yet at the same time as focusing on one strategy you have to have a bit of interest in each of the paths to victory or else you might be sneakily overtaken.

Strategy in this game is very fluid. If you play three games in a row, with the same people; you will have to change strategy each time, because your opponents will have noticed your previous strategy and seek to stymie you. For this reason, this game has high replay value.

Some have commented how boring the board is. I simply adore the map and would be happy to see something similar framed on a wall.

It scales quite well, though the more players you have, the busier the board gets and the more anxious One gets in seeking points. The scaling is helped by the double sided board which accommodates the different numbers of players.

The rules can seem a little dense at first, but when you get used to it (which won't take long) play proceeds quickly.

RATING: 9.7
[June 2017 - 9.7]
Another game that is just pure elegance and now ranks near the top of my list. The rules are fairly simple and straightforward, but the strategy is a lot more nuanced and hard to explain. It's a lot more about adapting and doing what the others aren't to maximize your engine before the rest of the players notice.  

[April 2017 - 9.5]
Still moving up.

[September 2015 - 9.4]
Another big step up in the rankings after playing a couple of times with players that know the game and keep the turns moving. Ditto on everything I've said previously. Even when I lose, I enjoy this game -- it feels like learning something new each time.

[July 2015 - 8.7]
Hansa takes a big jump up after a few more plays. Similar to my thoughts on Quantum, the more you play this game, the more you start to see all the different strategies. Every move seems to have pluses and minuses and I love the subtle interactions with other players in each move you make. It's that perfect balance of interaction and solitaire technology building. What the others do matter and can be sort of a "take that," but it's not too debilitating (at least on a single turn), there's always something else to do, if you're being blocked or displaced. And being displaced might even be the best thing that can happen to you in this game because of the compensation.  

[February 2015 - 7.6]
As a lot of people have noted, there's a lack of theme here ... but at least the board is beautiful to look at. I really like that as you play more, you learn how each ability can be pretty powerful, and you adjust based off what your opponents are doing. While everyone might rush for extra actions, there are ways to be a contrarian and gain points off that, or focus on other areas that will later be of value. It really is a game of markets. All the different ways to score points make it a little tough to teach, but after one full game, most should get the hang of it. This game might climb the ranks the more I play it.

Surely, they had to go and really try hard on purpose to make it so uninspiring, boring, and soulless. I cannot imagine any other explanation. So, essentially, it's just some cube pushing for the cube pushing sake, it doesn't make any sense whatsoever thematically. I'm amazed by this game's popularity - if you want to see how a similar ideas can be done properly, look no further than Age of Industry by Martin Wallace. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is definitely a Euro in every sense of the word.  At first, I thought it was completely abstract, but similar to Tigris & Euphrates, there is a sense in which the theme works when you look at it form a high level.  Each rule is very abstract, but taken as a whole, you do get the sense that you're a businessman / politician vying for power by helping and hindering your opponents / alliances.

While Hansa Teutonica has a mix of many mechanics we've seen before (getting extra actions, placing cubes on maps, making route connections, area control), the feel of the game is very unique.  Perhaps it is a little bit like Notre Dame, if I had to make a comparison to another game, except HT has player interaction in the form of direct conflict.

There is a 1 game learning curve, because there are many different ways to score points.  You get a few points here, a few points there, and a few points you didn't even realize you were going to get.  The winner ends up with 40 points to someone else's 38 points (or something like that).  

Because of the multiple ways to score, there are multiple strategies that accompany the scoring options.  Furthermore, each of these strategic options can either propel the game forward towards its end, or help stall the game to make it last longer.  Some strategies call for a quick game, and other strategies call for a long game.  Learning how to manipulate the length of the game while tactically improving your position is the crux of Hansa Teutonica. 

The game is very compelling.  I found myself thinking about the game after my first play.  I wanted to play again immediately.

RATING: 7
The board hurts my eyes, but there is a pretty good game here.  Really this comes down to an economic engine style game.  Take early moves to increase the power of future moves (like getting more actions or cubes back).  Take late moves to score VPs.  The good thing is there are a variety of ways to score VPs.  So the VP progression has some good choices.  However, the early game is about increasing your action count.  I'm not sure a player can score well if he falls behind on actions. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Hansa Teutonica is my favorite game to be released in 2009, and clearly one of my favorite games period.

What makes it stand out for me is the elegant interactions that game allows between players, as players are constantly competing for technologies, kontors, and game length. Individual players are incentivized to claim routes, while other players clearly would like to stop them. Rather than making this a zero sum affair, the designer implemented a genius action point system, where removing a player simply costs the displacing player some of their action points in exchange for giving the displaced player additional board presence, and a potential reduction in the number of actions required to claim a particular route somewhere else on the board. 

Because of this, and other similar mechanics, there are very few zero sum aggressive actions in the game, making it so there is always a question of whether displacing an opponent is more helpful to the displacing player or the displaced player. Is getting this increased technology worth giving your opponent additional points and giving them greater control over the game length? Did your opponent place that piece because they wanted to work towards claiming that route, or is it simply a distraction as they prepare for claiming another route in one turn?
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Players are doing ... something to do with the Hanseatic League.  Ultra dry cube placement game, where players are trying to link up two cities on the board to either form a network of trade routes, or improve their action ability by connecting certain cities.  Improvement means they get to do more later in the game with the same actions, while networking is victory point investment for late game scoring.  Nice, clean game that moves along at a nice pace.  Lots of different strategies to try - a large game space to explore via a simple set of rules.  However, you do need to get into the actions or perish.  Very good game.

RATING: 5
This is 110% pure Eurogame, without a doubt.  I see a mish-mash of very familiar concepts borrowed from El Grande, Blue Moon City, Thurn & Taxis, Notre Dame, and the like.   Roads are claimed by placing influence markers, and when completed, they are wiped clear and cities at the end points are claimed.   Or, you can claim bonuses instead of the cities.   There was a little twist in that shapes are important (round or cubes) but the game feels very derivative for the most part and pretty dry.  It has some back-scratching too, which isn't my favorite mechanic.  The graphic design is weak too (not to mention the worst cover art since Hamburgum), as the bonus scrolls are not clearly linked to the corresponding roads, and they are very small. I suppose it is a solid influence-placing game if you are into that sort of thing, but I don't think it is a game that will have much legs.   Need more plays to see about that.

RATING: 6.8
Not a bad route-building, positioning-style game.  It takes a little while to wrap your head around, since you have to figure out the value of the different upgrades available and decide which to focus on.  Then you've got to decipher the blocking aspect and learn how to figure out where other people might go, so you can block them.  Next, how you can fake people out to get the routes you want w/out being blocked, or when to bite the bullet and kick them off your route.
There's a lot to it, with many avenues to victory.  The game can end in a hurry, though, especially if a couple of the players are pushing for it, and not all of the long-term strategies seem like they have a good chance of paying off.  The network bonus, in particular, looks a little underpowered.  I don't think I've ever seen someone max out keys, and if you're doing that, you have to put out so many cities on the board to make use of it, which means you need to unlock at least some colors, and mb a disc…
I'm concerned that there are times when someone will get out to too much of a lead.  If you fail to do enough blocking, and someone gets ahead in actions per turn, they can pretty much go around the board taking what they want.  The person with 5 actions per turn won't always win, but they will if the game goes long enough.  If you haven't been positioning to shut it down quickly, you could be out of luck.  
It still feels like this game has more to be explored, which suggests it has good depth despite the static nature of the board/setup.

RATING: 7.32
This is hard core cube pusher euro game in every way. We are merchants/traders who are scoring routes between cities of Hansiatic League, which is middle age Germany. There are many many roads to victory, spending choice of 5 different actiones every turn. Abilities on player boards are upgraded Terra Mystica or Eclipse style which is awesome. On negative side, graphic design and iconography is not very helpful. Also static map mitigates its replayability in a long term. 
Recommended with yodelling music in the background.

RATING: 9.5
A truly, truly wonderful game. The special thing about this one is that you have incredibly many choices in every move. When other players try to block you or play against you, you still have zillions of other pretty good choices. So you're not easily frustrated, and it's much harder to team up against one player. It's harder to identify bad moves though, but even that helps you to not feel bad. And the major twist is that by kicking out an opponent's cube they get an additional cube, so hurting others also means helping them. It's a [b]feel-good[/b] abstract cube-pushing game, and this is quite an achievement. And it must be one of the most balanced games around, it seems like there is nothing that gets even close to a killer strategy.
It's true what people say that you might not want to play it with AP-prone folks.
But most important: Don't judge it simply from reading the rules, or from just looking at the board and pieces, I'd even say don't judge it from observing others playing it. It feels so different actually playing it yourself.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Hansa Teutonica feels more like a multiplayer abstract than a typical Euro.  Can it be compared to another game?  If I had to, I think it bares a very slight resemblance to Endeavor.  That said, I found Endeavor to be boring.

So what's the game about?  It's basically a multiplayer abstract game where you play discs and cubes to routes on the board between cities.  Once you exclusively have your pieces on the complete route, you may activate it.  When you activate a route, this will cause the route to empty but give you the benefit.  This will usually be one of three things: (1) Gain a bonus tile which gives you a special one-time benefit and will also give you bonus points at the end of the game. (2) Advance yourself in one of 5 'techs' which will increase your capability to influence the board. (3) Add a cube/disc to a city, which increases your network, can cause you to gain VP in future route activations as well as end game scoring, and sometimes an immediate benefit.

RATING: 8.5
-Nice balance of long-term strategy and tactical play resulting in a high degree of replayability.  
-Analysis paralysis potential seems relatively low.   Three player game took 60 minutes.  That's huge bang for your playtime buck!
-Variable end game conditions that seem effective in countering specific strategies.
-Novel gameplay despite being a fairly pasted Euro.  Somehow the designers have managed to throw together familiar mechanics in a way that feels unlike any Euro I've played to date.  
-Rather abstract in nature (with very little luck) but avoids being dry owing to five upgrade "technology" tracks, all of which seem vital depending on board position.  
-Confrontation for those who don't typically like a lot of direct  confrontation (like me).   In fact, setting yourself up to have your pieces attacked/displaced by your opponents is necessary not only to hinder your opponents but to gain benefits as well.  This encourages offensive and defensive play without causing irritation for those who don't like excessive conflict.

RATING: 7
Several things make this game stand out:  the wonderful choices between position and tech improvement, the symbiotic aspect of blocking, the bonus chits which aren’t just diversions but greatly influence your decisions, and the different ways to end the game. However, there's a barrier to entry that almost dissuaded me from holding onto the game.  You and your group need to grow together in skill else sub-par play can tilt the game one way early on and give a player to their left an advantage.

The HT death spiral usually begins with the start player who gets into the action track first and frequently; the rest of the table rushes to catch up to this person in actions (no! just go do something else!) and he benefits from them bumping him when they should move their cubes or let them sit there, block the damn route, and play chicken.  When this subtlety is lost, the butterfly effects mean one player jumps out ahead and then someone has to take one for the team to stop the leader in terms of not letting one player get all the bonus tiles (and then set down the next bonus tile on their route) or allow them to set the game tempo (short game vs long game). 


=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
Hansa Teutonica is my favorite game.  The almost-abstract feel to its play makes for an engaging and complicated game which I have not found elsewhere.

Why Hansa Teutonica is great:
1.  Extremely well balanced paths of victory.  Your actions directly affect how well the other players will do.  Are you clogging up the action spaces so nobody can have them?  Pushing for that Arnheim-Stendal connection?  Maybe you're just taking the Coellen Victory Points.

2.  Because the game is well balanced, you must constantly react to situations as they arise.  This is the most difficult and rewarding aspect.  Your opponent can completely screw up your plans, just because they want to.  You'll then have to decide whether that was worth it, or maybe choose a different option altogether.  The other game I love the reactive nature to is Race for the Galaxy; work with what you're dealt, as best you can.

3.  People love talking through the strategy, and meta-game is created and destroyed constantly.  New players tend to overvalue actions, groups go in and out on how they feel about networks, etc.  It's a game that begs to be 'solved', and is nearly impossible to.

If you want a dry themeless euro with a ton of interaction, and difficult choices, this is your game.  Not advised to play with people who have significant Analysis Paralysis, or it would take 2.5 hours.

RATING: 3.5
I didn't care very much for this game.  In addition to feeling fairly snowbally, it also felt like it was far too encouraging of players being a dick in contesting the extra actions section of the board.  By getting ahead on those actions, it became easier to crowd into that space, forcing other players to give you tons of free cubes on the board if they wanted a piece of that action, potentially accelerating your own engine while slowing everyone else's down.  I didn't care for the bonus tiles that gave massive numbers of extra actions as a one-shot, either.

This game pretty strongly disagreed with me - most games with dickery and conflict don't seem to so strongly force you into engaging in dickery if you don't explicitly choose to do so, but in this one it felt basically inevitable, and not in a light and silly "take that" sort of way, but in a "ugh I can't believe we're still playing this" sort of way.

I doubt I will play this one again.

RATING: 8
Neither the box nor the board of Hansa Teutonica look very exciting. Standard euro fare, one of about a million similar games. Same about the theme or the mechanics: Route building in medieval Germany for fame and profit. Placing your "workers" on roads enables you to score them and place one of them in a city instead which extends your trade network. There's more (bonus chits, a personal player board with upgrades, ...) but that's the gist of it, and you've seen it all before. Pretty much the only non-standard mechanic is that you can displace opponents' pieces from roads (at a cost, and with benefits for the displaced player).

Yet, the way all those little cog wheels fit together makes for a very intriguing game. Plays can run along very different paths with three different game end conditions, and players can focus on scoring points during the game or in the end game scoring. You can try to improve your personal board and make the game last longer, or you can ramp up your score quickly and try to end the game soon.

The complete package is what makes Hansa Teutonica compelling. Awesomeness in a box.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
Fünf simple Aktionen münden in das beste, härteste Verdrängungswettkampfspiel seit Bestehen der Deutschen Hanse. Bei HANSA TEUTONICA stimmt einfach alles: die kreative Idee, der Look, die Quintessenzialität, der Independent-Faktor, die Materialbescheidenheit. Vor allem Letzteres: aus einer Handvoll Holzklötzchen ein derart dichtes, intensives, konfrontierendes und einzigartiges Spielgefühl zu zaubern ist ein Geniestreich, den der Autor Andreas Steding wohl genauso wenig auf dem eigenen Schirm hatte wie Geburtshelfer Roman Mathar von Argentum und der dem kleinen Kölner Hinterhofverlag 2010 zum ersten Mal in Essen Scheinwerferlicht bescherte. HANSA TEUTONICA ist dabei mit keinem anderen Brettspiel vor und danach zu vergleichen, obwohl es sich formal als Setzspiel in die Schublade stecken lässt. Aber selbst innerhalb dieses Clusters bleibt HANSA TEUTONICA ein charakterstarkes, völlig individuelles Kunstwerk, das Raum und Zeit des Brettspiels einmal von West einmal ungeachtet gegen jeden Markt eigenwillig und ungeschliffen durchkreuzt. Selbst das abrupte, abreissende Spielende passt nahtlos zum Wesen dieses taktischen Meisterwerks. Noch einmal: HANSA TEUTONICA ist faktisch einfach nur langweilig Klötzchen setzen. Aber die Fülle an Bewegung, an Dynamik, an Interaktion, Blockade, Stellungskrieg und Varianz in Offensive und Defensive versetzt mich bis heute in das gleiche Erstaunen und Entzücken wie in dem Moment als es bei mir bei HANSA TEUTONICA das erste Mal Klick machte und sich alles erhellte. Das nennt man wohl Erleuchtung.

RATING: 8
HT is a damn strange game to wrap your head around during the rules explanation, but it actually flows quite well when you get into it. It's quite a punishing game though. Even with all that in mind, it's such a well constructed game that it is truly intriguing to play and get a fix on things. For that reason, I quite enjoy it, even with all the punishment.  I really appreciate what the designers have done here.

RATING: 8
This one is growing on me.  The player interaction is very in-your-face, actually providing incentive for players to get in each other's way.  I have seen multiple paths to victory, which speaks well to the game balance.

The two player variant is rather clever.  The players have access to the entire board, but they must move a "pawn" token to the area of the board where they would like to carry out actions.  The "attraction" of the pawn leads to quite a bit of conflict between the two players, preventing the game from turning into "two player solitaire".

RATING: 5
What a lifeless game. There are lots of different things to go for, and different ways to score points. But the whole thing is so completely abstract, and yet somehow so complicated. Most games with this many different options will tie more closely to some theme, while simpler games tend to have less theme. But not here. 

I'm not sure how much of my tepid reaction to the game is due to the rules explanation I got, which was stripped of even a token reference to the theme. I think it helps to grasp a game as a whole to have some overview of what the game is about, even if the theme is very thin. It helps to have some sort of framework to hold the different pieces together, instead of just memorizing that "keys multiply connected cubes" and "bags let you draw more cubes."

Although the game is a fairly standard cube pusher, oddly enough I haven't played a game like this in a long time.

RATING: 8
RECEIVED: 2016

HT always takes a while to teach but, after a turn or two, the game play breezes along. After all, it's merely place cubes, move cubes, take actions, or take offices. And the multiple scoring opportunities and the unlocked powers ramp play up very quickly. 

Although being the starting player does have advantages, particularly in getting the extra actions, there's also opportunity to rush the game by placing offices in strategic spots. 

Finally, I appreciate the balance of knowing when to switch from developing abilities to building your route. 

It's a clever game with a lot of options, despite the simple game play. And, most importantly for me for a Euro, HT has tons of player interaction. Lastly, it never overstays its welcome.

RATING: 8
I was not expecting much out of Hansa Teutonica.  Although the board is beautiful in its own way, the game had the aura of a lifeless, mechanical game for humorless German nerds.  Well, yes, I did find it nerdy and very mechanical and very, very, very German.  But not lifeless.  What gives it life are the tense standoffs and move/countermove interactions among the players, along with the complex and intertwined strategic paths that open up as you begin to understand what is possible.  This is a subtle, sophisticated strategy game in a plain brown paper wrapping.

Hansa Teutonica is a good and impressive game, but I don't yet know whether it is the game for me.  I'm always wary of games with nothing random to "shake up" the setup or the gameplay.  If a game starts to feel too same-y, that's usually when I lose interest.

[UPDATE] Yes, I'm still enjoying it, and it's not yet feeling same-y. Plus now there's an alternate board to help allay those concerns.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Absolutely dry game, with no connection between theme and mechanics: place and move cubes and discs on the board while managing your little spreadsheet. There is very little randomness but a lot of annoying chaos. The gameplay is tedious and repetitive. In my only play, after 15 minutes I was bored to death and didn't care for the game anymore, but the suffering lasted almost two hours.

This is one of those games that try to create an illusion of depth by offering so many ways to score that it is hard to keep everything in mind and evaluate the best course of action. However, going for extra actions early is crucial, and the players who manage to get them will leave the others in the dust. To make things worse, having your cubes kicked off actually gives you more cubes to place for free on the board, and if the other players just ignore you for the first few rounds, it may be very hard (impossible?) to catch up.

The graphic design is no better, confusing in some spots and with unintuitive iconography, not to mention the drab artwork.

Despite all that, I might give it another try with fewer players (I played with five), but I'm certainly not looking forward to do it. I was more annoyed than entertained.

RATING: 10
Excellent euro.  Plays fast and has great decisions throughout.  You must be able to adapt your strategy and use all of the tools you have to win. Because blocking is integral to the gameplay, you will be blocked - often.  Just go with it and have a back up plan.  

The rule book is poorly done.  It can be a chore to learn but once you do, it is worth it. Print out a player guide from the files section.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
For a medium-light game, the rules are a little overwhelming -- there are a bunch of special cases.  And the fact that you have to pay attention both to the number of dots between cities (2, 3, or 4 makes a big difference), and to the number and types of actions that your opponents can take on their turns means that there is a lot to consider on every turn.  Ultimately, this is a game where you have to work hard to foil other players' plans while still achieving your own.  The only way to learn how to do this is to play... so it's not an easy game to teach, but one that can be learned after a game of getting one's plans foiled by others.

Interesting that there is almost no randomness (except for the bonus chips).

There seem to be many ways to win.  In my first play, I won with 61 points in a 4-player game because I picked up the 11- and 9-point merchant spots in the lower left city and had decent (but not great) city connectivity and 4-point advancements on two privilege tracks.  I only had 3 actions per turn when all others had 4 or 5, so it apparently isn't a requirement (although it would really have helped).  Other strategies include controlling many cities, building huge connected networks with bonus key multipliers, picking up many bonus action chips, and trying to end the game early by getting people to build near cities that you control.  A combination is best -- it depends a lot on the players in the game.

It's important to get lots of pieces on the board, because then even with few actions, you can move 2-5 pieces with a single move action and get lots of things done.  One way of increasing cubes on-board is to place a blocker... if someone needs to remove you, it costs them an extra cube, gives you an extra cube, and leaves you with more pieces on the board to play with.  But the Book track is really important to this strategy.

RATING: 5
This is my comment after 2 2 players games.  I will try with 4 players before barbecuing it.
I love euro games, really; I find them to be the superior breed of games, with the occasional (too rare indeed) ameri-style game that manages to be playable.
But Hansa Teutonica is for me the embodiment of the game that the fanatical hordes of ameritrashers loathe; it's a caricature of the less interesting parts of euro.  Endless tactical bickering, total divorce from theme (and I find that Tigris and Euphrates has a meaningful theme), and too many turns you feel you accomplish nothing.  The agression is there, but it's neither engaging like a good old battle, nor particularly rewarding.
If I want an abstract game, Id'rather play a streamlined affair like Keltis; I feel that Hansa Teutonica has a mockery of a theme only to justify it's needlessly complicated engine, but neither of them makes any fun.
Of course my impressions of a game are influenced by my expectations, and after doing my research I was convinced Hansa Teutonica was a game for me.  It was definitely not.  Sure the game is fast, but I was astonished that it lasted only one hour; it felt much more.
