=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Where did this come from? How is it that this slipped under the radar in 2009? This is an excellent game, with many interesting choices, tense tactical play, and thought-provoking strategic play.

It seems similar in feel to the following games:
* Notre Dame -- traders/merchants (the cubes) need to be brought from the "stock" to "personal supply".
* Thurn & Taxis -- need to establish routes between any two cities in Germany (almost same geographical area), to establish offices (to get points from other players who connect the same two cities you already did), or to get specific upgrades to your menu of actions.
* Ticket to Ride -- need to establish longest continuous route of offices (but not driven by any destination cards).

There are only five possible actions:
1) place a cube from personal supply to a route;
2) claim completion of a route between two cities, and
a) get an action upgrade (if one is available), OR
b) establish an office on either end of the route (if available), AND
c) get a "bonus" token that may give you additional actions or different type of action not available in the menu;
3) displace an opponent's cube (which has a cost for you and a benefit for your opponent);
4) move X number of cubes and transfer them to other parts of the map; and
5) move Y number if cubes down from stock to personal supply.

You start the game with 2x actions, but this can be slowly upgraded up to 5x actions by completing a specific route.

Scoring starts out painfully slow, with 1 pt being awarded to player(s) who have the highest-ranking office on both ends of a completed route. Since routes can be completed a number of times, by various players, the offices can garner these incremental points. It is also possible to get 7 points by connecting the two main cities on the edges of the board with a continuous route of offices. Once somebody gets 20 of these points (or 10x cities have filled out their office spaces), the game is over. However, the real scoring then begins, with pts being awarded for:
1) completion of specific action upgrades;
2) specific point claims on the board (I don't remember what these were);
3) 2 pts for having the highest-ranking office in each city;
4) 2 pts per office on the board (only one player's office per city);
5) 1 pt per bonus token collected;
6) N-pts for the player's longest chain of offices TIMES the number of "keys" (another upgrade item) multipliers they have collected.

Thus, just because somebody has scored the game-ending 20 pts, doesn't mean they will necessarily win. This is the exciting aspect of the game, since there are various paths to victory. Since the 20 pts serves as one of the game clocks (the other two being totally maxing out the office spaces in 10 cities and total depletion of bonus markers), this makes the game length variable. However, there is very little downtime, as the decision tree is relatively small (you only have two actions to begin with, the question is what to do, and where to do it?), the game clips along at a rapid pace, clocking in at 90-120 minutes. The sweet spot may be 4 players, as 5 players introduces more chaos, like a 6 player game of Acquire.

There is also the subtle difference that there are cubes and discs, representing traders and merchants. You start out with a lot of traders, but with only one merchant (more can be had by upgrading). However, the merchants are more expensive to displace, and in some cities they are required to lock up an office space. The office spaces are also color-coded, so upgrades to "privilege" must be made to occupy those higher-ranking slots.

Given that players can displace each other in competition for key routes, the game plays like "... a knife-fight in a phone booth." The game could change dramatically in one round, if players have upgraded their menu of actions (and number), so it makes for a tense, nail-biting game. In my opinion, this is a much better and more satisfying game than Notre Dame, especially since one doesn't have to deal with rats and there are virtually no random elements (except the bonus markers), or the "screw your neighbor" aspect of passing action cards--you know exactly what actions you are entitled to. Further, it is far superior to Thurn & Taxis given the high level of interaction, and more fun than TTR since there is "combat", if you can call it that, to claim dominance of specific routes. The only quibble is that the board is fixed (with the placement of bonus markers being the only random element), and everything else is perfect-information, so the game openings will eventually succumb to group-think, and re-playability will suffer. Thus, I'm glad to hear that there are new expansion boards coming out.

Initially a '9', but I expect it to easily go to '10' when I get my own copy. Impressive design, with great integration of several different (and novel) mechanisms. Definitely a winner.

Update (1/4/11) 2 plays: Looks like the keys bonus scoring was incorrect: Multiply the key level by the N total number of connected and contiguous offices. The cities don't need to be a single continuous chain like in Ticket To Ride, but is the equivalent to "largest network" in Railroad Tycoon. Plus, multiple cubes/discs in each city are counted too. This makes the keys more powerful than previously thought. However, I wonder if there is any strong incentive to set up offices early in the game, rather than constantly fighting over the skill upgrade routes.

Update (1/14/11) 4 plays: Game is getting a lot of play at the meetups and at home, as it has shot up in popularity. Played the 3 player board, which is more fluid (much less downtime), but also less tension and chaos. The game seems to be best with 5 players, but downtime is then a few extra seconds of agony and anxious waiting, thus 4 players being the sweet spot in terms of tension and downtime. The game is easy to explain, although there are many small rule details which can be easily overlooked. Further, we have uncovered 3 or 4 rule errors in the English translation, so caution is advised. Aw, who am I kidding? This is a '10', through and through.

Update (10/10/11): Has fallen as quickly as it has shot up. People grumble when I recommend this game, as they are not interested in watching two/three-way pissing contest down around the city that awards additional actions; they have concluded that maxing out on the number of actions is the key to the game. I have seen other strategies win, but it looks like this game needs time away from the table. Downing to an '8' for the time being.

2.鑰匙分:最後最長的工會X分數，新增進的能力可以在當回合立即使用。
3.踢別人小商人自己會損失一個，對方可以從供應區拿一個到場上擺放，踢大商人1個會支付數量等於2,被踢的玩家必須將方塊放到相鄰的道路上，在下一個行動前。若被踢的人銀行內已沒有任何標記，他可以從個人供應區拿取標記；如果個人供應區也沒有標記，他可以直接移動圖板上的標記。
4.道路完成的分時，有最多公會的玩家有控制權，如果平手，公會開在最右邊的玩家有控制權。
5.將賞標記
如果道路有連接獎賞標記，宣告道路完成的玩家可以獲得該獎賞標記。
不能在獲得獎賞標記的行動中立刻使用，若有下一個行動可以在該行動使用。
將補充的獎賞標記放在任意一條道路上，此道路必須是空的、至少要有一相鄰且包含公司格的都市、且沒有其他的獎賞標記在上面。將標記的箭頭對準道路。
6.Standal到Arnheim的城市鍊(紅色城市名稱)，首位達成的玩家可以得到7分、第二位4分、第三位2分。
7.遊戲結束:有人20分，獎賞用完，第十個城市。
直接得分:完成道路、公會建立分數，SA道路
最後計分:能力發展完4分，CW分數，控制城市2分，城市鍊X鑰匙

RATING: 6
Players use a limited number of actions per turn to earn VP during and at the end of the game. Mostly actions involve putting ones own traders and merchants on roads between cities (possibly displacing other people's pieces). If you manage to occupy an entire road (usually 3 or 4 pieces) then you can use an action to take off all your pieces and either place one piece in one of the two cities or in the case of some cities or roads gain an alternative advantage.

Quite what this taking your pieces off roads once they are full has to do with the theme of medieval merchants is not clear.  It is also unclear which of the several ways to score points are more viable nor is it clear at the beginning of the game how many turns the game will last or how many turns or actions it will take to earn points by each method.  Hence viable strategies are quite obscure.

RATING: 9.3
Un juego abstracto obtenido de lo que resultaría al mezclar Goa o Diamonds Club + Thurn und Taxis o Taj Mahal.

Los jugadores son mercaderes de la liga Hanseática intentando ganar prestigio gracias a abrir oficinas comerciales, mejorar sus capacidades, montar redes de rutas comerciales y ganar fichas de bonus. Suena a juego económico, pero no es así. Tampoco de colocar trabajadores (a lmenos exclusivamente). Básicamente es un juego de situar mercaderes y comerciantes (cubos) en rutas del mapa para reclamar rutas. Lo distinto es que, una vez completada la ruta, se ejecuta una acción asociada a ella y se retiran los cubos. De este modo, una misma ruta, se puede reclamar más veces durante la partida. Alternativamente, en vez de ejecutar la acción asociada, podrás mejorar una de tus 5 habilidades.

Me gusta mucho la variedad de formas de conseguir PV's: por losetas de bonus (los platos de comida), por situar oficinas, por completar rutas en las que tengas oficinas, la puntuación final por las oficinas que controlas, la red de oficinas más larga, las habilidades desarrolladas al máximo, la conexión entre dos ciudades específicas del mapa y los puntos obtenidos en otra ciudad específica del mapa ("los barriles")

También me gustan las distintas condiciones gracias a las que se dispara el final del juego: cuándo alguien anota 20 PV's o más, al completar 10 ciudades o cuándo se agotan las losetas de bonus ("los platos").

PROS: Los novatos lo suelen pillar enseguida. Buen equilibrio entre estrategia a largo plazo y oportunidades tácticas a corto, lo que genera gran rejugabilidad. Ausencia casi completa del azar. CONS: Muy abstracto y con el tema absolutamente pegado. Repetitivo. Pretende crear la ilusión de ser profundo ofreciendo muchas formas de sumar PV's, para que sea dificil evaluar cuál es la mejor opción en cada momento.

[ENG]

An abstract game obtained from a blend of three games: Goa (or Diamonds Club) + Thurn und Taxis (or Taj Mahal).

The players are traders from Hanseatic League trying to increase their prestige level. To do this they need to open sales offices, improving their skills, developing trading routes and obtaining bonus tiles. All of this sounds like a economical game, but it isn´t, neither a worker placement game (well, at least is not ONLY a worker placement game). Basically it´s a game about placement of traders and merchants (cubes) on the map routes and claim them. The different part is that you can claim a route more than one time during the game. Alternatively, inestead of executing the associated action, you can increase one of your five skills.

I do love the different ways to obtain VP´s: using bonus tiles (food dishes), placing offices, completing routes where you have offices, the end score in offices that you control at the end of the game, the longest office´s net, developing skills to the maximum level, the connection between two specific towns of the mal and the points obtained in a specific city of the map ("the barrels").

I do love too the different conditions that trigger the end of the game: when a player has 20 VP´s (o more), when the bonus tiles are run out or when ten cities are completed.

PROS: the rookie players get the system quickly. There is a good balance between long term strategy and short term choices, so it generates a lot of replayability. There´s no luck. CONS: Pretty abstract game with pasted theme. Repetitive. It aims to create the illusion of depth game offering many ways to score VP's, to make it difficult to assess what is the best choice at each moment.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is a very interesting Euro game, which feels utterly unique among other games I have played.  The basic core, route completion, is, I guess, similar to games like Ticket To Ride, but everything around it is much more strategic.  One thing that's interesting to me is that, like other Euro games, there are many paths to victory.  However, unlike other games, the level of player interaction here and the different victory conditions means that there are many directions that the [i]game[/i] itself will take.  Some games might go quickly with everyone rushing for bonus markers.  Others might go more slowly with everyone increasing their skills.  One or more players focusing on a given strategy (lots of actions, big network, lots of bonus markers, etc) might require a strong response in the strategies from other players, such that everyone has to work against that focused player (I think).  

The more I play this, the more depth reveals itself. It's possibly too chaotic with all the player interaction to allow very deep, long-term strategy, but it's certainly a game that rewards study and practice.

RATING: 8
A very good THEMELESS Euro, not that that is something new in Euro games but it is nice when the theme actually matters. 

So quite dry really but everything (else) you want a Euro to be is there which is the important part.

Fast moving, multiple scoring/paths to take, very low luck and its strategic and seems to have some depth. Extra actions do seem to be overpowered compared to the other options so fight for them early, and the cubes help a lot too.  

Only played once so far, rating could rise a bit like 8.5, not sure it could be a 9 for me, possibly.   

[u]Update:[/u] Finally played a second time about a year later, the dryness and soulessness really does hurt the game for me and many others BUT the damn game itself is very good and I might just have to pick it up. With some kind of spark or theme this would be an easy 9 for me.    

The main reasons I am tempted to buy it are: 

 - VERY fast turns; really NO AP what-so-ever which is nice for a game with options and some depth and not usually the case. Also games should be 90 minutes. 

 - VERY low luck; mostly just some PIC (player chaos) but even that has fairly minimal effects so overall low

 - Muliptle paths; very open-ended and several ways to skin the cat here, will be dependant on players too which keeps the game replayable (though a static map does not, hence need for expansion).  

So like my first game I really like it, but if ever there was a prime example of a Dry themeless souless cube pusher, this is it.

Plus it feels pretty quick to play since turns are so short that there's little downtime.

RATING: 10
Hansa Teutonica's biggest sin is a dry theme that feels out of place. Otherwise, it's an engrossing experience filled with strategic decisions centered around choosing to play the long-game by investing in more powerful abilities or the short-game by scoring points as quickly as possible. These strategies are executed via quick turns of tactical maneuvering that encourage constant interaction but not aggressive destruction of opponents. Each game plays a bit differently every time based on the way each player plans and reacts, giving the game a great deal of replayability. It's a game that is close to perfection.

RATING: 6
Hansa Teutonica gave a pretty good first impression. It's definitely a very old-school eurogame, complete with a graph-like map whereupon players place cubes in order to claim trade routes, establish presence in cities, etc. A private player board is used to track your progress on five different development tracks, each allowing for more efficient play options as they are advanced.

A couple of thoughts: The board is static and it seems as if a couple of specific routes, which allow for special actions, will tend to play a central role in each and every game. I fear this may influence the staying power of the game in a bad way.

The game actually has a lot in common with Endeavor, but in my book Endeavor comes away as the clear winner in almost all respects.

2014-10-14

Initial Rating: 5.0 (October 2014)

I went to great pains to acquire a copy of Hansa Teutonica, in fact I bought Hansa thinking it was this game. When I was reading the rules I thought the game was going to be fantastic AND it is one of the best ranked games here on BGG, as of today it is number 66 in the general ranking. So I was very excited to try it and sadly it disappointed me. However I still have some hopes for the game.

First of all the rules are hard to understand from the rulebook, I’m not sure why, perhaps the way they are redacted or something, but I had to re-read them many times to ensure I actually understood them. They aren’t hard to get once we began playing the game (I can explain the game under 12 minutes now) so I think they could be much clearer. Besides, now I know that the rules I read have 3 mistakes, so we played wrong the final scoring, I didn’t understand what was the “office network” so I need to give it another chance soon.

The gameplay was very fast, in fact we finished our game very quickly but most of the map was empty during the game and we always fought for the same routes, namely the one that gives the players more actions. The game ended very quickly via VPs, the other end conditions seem very hard to accomplish. I have only played a 3 player game and I thought it would be a lot tighter because it was the maximum number of players for that map.

The theme is very boring and inexistent. The art is very regular too. The cover of the box induces sleep in everyone who gazes at it. 

Bottom line, I’m disappointed. I will give it another chance soon with the correct scoring this time.

RATING: 8
There's no doubt in my mind that Hansa Teutonica is a very good game.  The extortion mechanic is brilliant.  I'm not sure I like it, but it breathes new life into what in other games is predictable blocking.

Variable player powers through technologies are also a great deal of fun, and it's nice to see more Euros incorporating them.

I never looked at the 2-3 player board but it's nice to see with HT and Small World a growing awareness within the modern game design community that modifying the board layout with increasing player counts can be every bit as important as modifying starting resource positions.

More than anything my reaction to Hansa Teutonica highlights how much I've changed in my gaming tastes.  Back in 2006 I would have rated the game a 9 and played it 10-15 times.  Now I'm not sure I have the patience for repeatedly playing this style of game anymore.  It's not quite Ra in its theme binding but let's be honest, the game doesn't exactly make me feel like I'm traipsing through the lowlands of northern Germany with my haversack and my merchant's guile.  And there were certain mechanical elements which were never going to appeal to me that much, like the blocking (as interesting as this game makes it) and the network building without anything to deliver over said network.

I need to give this game another couple plays.  I was the only newbie at the table and didn't really know what I was doing, so it's possible some of my negative feelings amounted to frustration.  That should go away with experience.  It's also possible that playing any new game as my first game 16 hours after my all-time favorite (and a very different beast) Advanced Civilization is setting it up for failure with me.  If I can muster enough enthusiasm to make it 5-10 plays with Hansa Teutonica it will be well worthy of an 8.  Otherwise it will fall into the set of good games which just don't click for me.  There are enough that do click so that wouldn't be a great tragedy.

Update 7/2010:  3 plays in and I'm starting to see how this game works.  I'm much more kindly disposed toward the game than I was on first play.  I now rate it 8 without reservations, and I might consider a 9 after a few more plays.

RATING: N/A
As of January 2017, this is my most recently purchased game. After watching a playthrough video on Terra Mystica, I was intrigued when the video claimed that Terra Mystica shared some mechanisms with a game called Hansa Teutonica. I had no idea what that game was, so I then researched it, and it looks like a gem of a game. (I suppose the player board and how you increase/decrease your resources for the next round after you take a building off the map and the other back onto the player board in Terra Mystica is similar to how you take your cubes for your special abilities off the board and increase your ability in Hansa Teutonica.)
Although I am not really looking forward to reading the rulebook for Hansa Teutonica (I have heard that it can put you to sleep...), I am looking forward to figuring out the specifics of how it is played.
What I really like about what I have seen regarding this game is that it is very interactive and there is a lot of strategy as far as whether you want to kick someone off their route, but if you do, then that opponent will get compensated. So the question becomes is it really worth it to kick your opponents out?
This is the type of game that seems like it would play best with more players.
I cannot wait to start introducing it to my gaming group! (I can think of one person in particular who I believe will really like this game, because he likes another interactive area control type of game which has a mechanism where you kick your opponents out which I have played with him, called "Royals" (another good game)).

RATING: 9
Players are merchants trying to gain prestige in the Hansiatic League by proving their mettle as merchants in various ways - by opening trade offices, improving trade skills, building networks, and gaining special favors (bonus chips). This makes it sound like an economic game, but it is not. BGG describes the game has having an "action point allowance" system, which is not true either. Yes you start with two actions per turn and this can be increased, but its not really an "action point" system if every action costs one point. It is a route building game with a Goa-like "action improvement" system. BGG has not come up with a term for this sort of mechanic, but we really need to. It's quite distinct.

Basically, this is a game of placing traders and merchants onto routes in order the claim the route. What makes this game a bit different from other route-building games is that once you claim the route, you use it to perform an action associated with that route and then remove it. In this way a single route can be claimed many times in the course of a game. A persistent route or chain of routes is established by creating offices in cities at either end of a route as one of the possible actions to take. Alternatively, at some cities, instead of establishing an office you can improve in one of 5 skills that increases the power of standard actions. There's actually quite a bit of interdependency and subtlety in the various options that allows for both short term point grabs and long term strategies. It's an old-school euro mechanically but those mechanics come together in a way that fits perfectly with the more complex tendency of recent euros, while avoiding feeling stale and re-hashed. It's pretty impressive.

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Good route-building-engine-efficiency game. I dig how all the tech tracks funnel back to building and reenforcing your presence on the map. This helps to fight against the pull towards points-salad-lets-maximize-all-the-tracks thing. I like that.

Even better is the displacement action. Screwing your neighbor isn’t zero-sum but rather has an immediate cost to the attacker and potential benefit to the victim both immediately and in subsequent turns. I really like that.

But what makes it great is that if played well one can begin to not only incentivize others through cube positioning, and inviting displacement, but can start to manage those incentives in such a way as to determine the board state toward their benefit. Now that I love.

A couple of things I am unsure about: 

1) In both plays I had the most keys and largest network and won both times. Does that mean the game always devolves into a fight over the gaining or blocking the keys? 

2) The bonus tiles allow you to break the rules in various ways. While I’m okay with that in general, the free displacement tiles run contrary to the most interesting decision and dare I say spirit of the game?
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
My best "new-to-me" game of 2010!

A beautiful game design with a relatively pasted-on theme. The penalty for displacing other traders is a really nice and interesting feature.

There's a lot of talk about the first moves being scripted, but I don't mind that (after all, everyone knows e4 in chess, so what? ); I still think it should be played out, b/c that initial fight between 3 players is still interesting, and there are still decisions to be made by the 4th and 5th players; so starting at 3 actions would take away that part of game. 

Bottom line, this is one of those games that sticks in your mind long after the play, causing you to wonder - what if I try this? Or that? These are the kinds of games I'm in this hobby for.

I absolutely love the "medieval map" board, which is commented on and compared (favorably) to authentic medieval european maps here: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/507289/like-the-fugger-welser-medici-board

Other similar style boards from medieval times:

http://www.wilfriedbluhm.de/Landkarten/
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
[b]Thank You Secret Santa 2010![/b]

I think I've figured out why people are responding so positively to this Dark Horse title from Essen. It doesn't feel like a new release. It is not a complex, multi-layered, heady blend of several other well-known heavy games. Instead it feels like it could easily have been released 10 years ago. Rather than having a vague sense of its potential after a full game, by turn two everything clicks and you think, "oh, this is really good."

Unfortunately, Hansa Teutonica sounds pretty dull on paper. All you do is compete for short city-to-city links on the board by placing cubes. Once you finish a link, you can either place a marker in an adjoining city, which will score you a few points depending on future board activity, or you can claim a "tech" advancement associated with that city. There are five types of advancements and several levels of each. All of them enable stronger boardplay.

In practice it's a snappy, satisfying game that combines straightforward rules with a sense of fluid, open-ended play. You choose how to develop your abilities as the game progresses. It also feels tight in that you must respond to other player's moves and continually obstruct their plans. The gameplay has all of the engagement and precision associated with blocking but none of the spite or tit-for-tat. You can always "unblock" yourself, you just pay a small cost to do so and give a small benefit to the blocker. So I'll get in your way because I don't want you to have easy access to a powerful advancement. You'll still get it, but it'll cost you a bit more and you'll have to help me a little elsewhere on the board.

It's fast. It's reasonably short (45m). Almost anyone should be able to grasp what's going on and get into it. Gamers like it. Eight-year olds like it. Who'd have thought?

RATING: 6
One of the Euriest Euros I've played; pushing and pulling cubes are the heart and soul of this game. Player representation is tenuous, a lot of abstract stuff just sort of happens, and the rules explainers actively resisted elaborating on theme and thematic justification. Score is conservatively positive after one game, a three-player game dominated by the action hogging strategy; I expect a four- or five-player game will bring out more of the game's qualities.

After two and a half years finally played a five-player game, and came in second after a runaway leader. I feel the game is lacking something: most of your turns are spent quickly placing a couple of cubes aiming for something and hoping to still be aiming for it when your next turn comes around, so that two or three turns down the line you can gain a bonus and start aiming for something else. Rating stays put for now, though.

But dropped the rating by 1 after a few more years. I'm just not thrilled by how the game turns on action upgrades and how engine upgrades in general feel like competitive water treading  with respect to the board state. Maybe the expansion map is just awesome though.

RATING: 10
For me, this game shines best with 5 seasoned hansa teutonica players going at one another during the game.

07/2016:
We had a debate after my latest game in which I rushed to a victory by emptying the supply of dinner plates. The debate was over whether or not a quick victory was as fulfilling or satisfying as a win in a long game. I always appreciated HT as a game with many diverse paths to victory, ,long and short. You always have to block the leader and seek out the path of least resistance. If you play long - you have to disrupt the plans of the 'rushers' collectively as a group. If you aim to win short, the clock is always ticking and you have to squeeze out the end game conditions before sheer number of actions by those playing the long game start to overwhelm you.

RATING: 7.5
Really abstract and just as ultra euro as it gets, both in look and feel.  Interesting mechanic with very simple rules, good flow and decent tactics.  Plays impressively fast (40 mins for a 3 players, 1h for 5 players).  Experience is sadly inferior with 4 because of the map, better with 3 and 5.  Because it plays so fast, the game can be quite hard to read and lots can happen very fast at the end, calling the winner.  Viable long term planning is pretty much useless because of high player interaction (which is why it's fast) but lots is happening on the tactical level.  Simple enough that it's getting old fast for me (personal).

RATING: 5
Some interesting/good ideas but too long and it outlasts it's welcome, for me personally.  I'd like it if it was under 2 hrs.  This is a middleweight game in the time category of a heavier game!  It's also too fiddly and too many layers that lengthens game time in excess (I'd suggest to take out one of the skill tracks and streamline some other layer like replacing the bonus chips in exchange for hidden goal/s card/s).  I was interested up until halfway through and wondered why is this not ending?  With certain groups, there will be lots of downtime or analysis paralysis.  After the first two hours, I thought the fun factor was diminishing severly and wish I was playing something else similar, but much faster (like "Via Romana").  Also, this game is highly prone to groupthink efficiency.  You want to go after things no else is competing with.  Without hidden goals or something like that, if two people go after the same strategy, the lagging player will be severely punished.  For the time it takes to play this, I'd rather be playing a heavier game with similar time length.  I think I understand all the strategies and options available but I just don't feel the fun after 2 hours, nor do I wish to go through the long grind again to find out.  Pound for pound it's simply not worth my time.  Bottom line, most of the game just didn't do it for me b/c I have issues with how they mesh.  I don't hate it, the game mostly "works" (with some issues), but you'd have to do a great negotiation job to talk me into trying it again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
An excellent game of both tactical and strategical implications as one develops a long term plan but sometimes must consider short term changes and implications.  Only five choices available for action choices make the game simple to play, but the interwoven aspects and many possibilities for how one wants to proceed towards the goal leads to considerable thought.  Some people have locked up at times for a while to think through how to optimize their turn and 2-4 actions.  One can develop four different tracks - one grants more actions on a turn, another allows you to move around more guys on the map, another grants you bonus multiplier for your largest cloud of connected cities, and the other determines how many guys you can bring into your "province" from the reserve (similar to El Grande).  Bonus chits throw in additional motivations to complete certain chits and allows you to place the new chit in a different location more favorable to help you and also allows you to bend some rules.  The beautiful thing about this game is the ability to pursue several different winning strategies and the fact that once you know how to play, the game can be played in 35-40 minutes.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
An unconventional area control game that interlocks a couple of different, but related, area control systems. In one, the roads, you must acquire exclusive control of the territory (possibly by bumping opposing pieces at the price of giving them more moves on an adjacent road), the sacrificing your control in exchange for one of a few possible rewards, including improving your efficiency at the various available actions or gaining influence in one of the cities at the ends of the road. Control over those cities gives you money (VPs) when any player "cashes in his chips" on an adjacent road.

It's all a bit confusing at first, and it's easy to latch onto the obvious plan of bumping how many actions you get to take each turn right out of the gate, but I now suspect that the game is just a tad more subtle than that. You're probably better off trying to set yourself up to take advantage of your opponents' plans first, and then work on developing some efficiencies of your own once the rush has died down a little and, hopefully, when you'll get a point or two out of the bargain as well.

My biggest concern going forward, my previous paragraph notwithstanding, is that I'm a little uncertain yet where the balance between strategy and tactics lies. Investing in cities is certainly a strategic move, at least on its face, and there is an infrastructure-building element (those efficiencies I mentioned) as well. Unfortunately, road play is very much catch-as-catch-can. When you're ready to play to a road, you have to look at what's available, and there's a decent chance that your first choice or two are likely to be at least partially blocked.

If another play or two come down on the side of strategy over tactics, Hansa Teutonica will be a must-buy for me. If it ends up being largely an exercise in in-the-moment tactics, I'll still play on occasion, given the opportunity, but I'm unlikely to pine for it.

Just let the bland abstractness wash over you and give in to the tidy strategic play...

(Upped this to a 9 Maybe a 10):  When I need a bland, nearly-luckless Euro fix, this where I go.  It has all the cube-pushing and barely pasted-on-theme goodness you could imagine without all the meaningless noisy distraction of excessive resource manipulation that seems so popular as of late.  It is also playable well under an hour, so doesn't overstay it's welcome.  It is distilled Euro par excellence.  *Cue "Bravo, Bravo!" and quiet, distinguished applause appropriate for a distinguished game such as this*  I still wish it had a theme that fit better with the mechanics and was more interesting.  But, then again, it would lose its bland Euro charm, I suppose.

Update (8):  conflict-heavy Euro that almost has a wargame feel.  Interesting gameplay developing.  Rating is going up as I accept and embrace the abstractness of the game.  This could become a favorite.

After first play (4):  Hansa Tuetonica is every bit as dense, abstract, unintuitive, dry, and derivative as its first impression.  I don't understand the buzz and hype at all.  The theme would be neutral at best, being yet another more or less medieval central European trading game.  Even worse the mechanics and rules really don't particularly suit the theme.  The rules are difficult to learn from reading them.  I think I would enjoy this more as a pure abstract, as the pasted-on theme is distracting and adds to the potential analysis paralysis for first time players.  There is just nothing that stands out except perhaps the blandness and unapproachability of this game.  Still, there's nothing quite like the rush of displacing a wooden colored cube or disc first thing in the morning.  So, perhaps upon further play I will revise my opinion.

RATING: 8
optimal balance between player interaction and scoring victory points
+ many paths to victory ; there are no preprogrammed victory paths ; everything depends on the moves of the other players. 
There are many player dependend bottle necks in this game ; are you going for the race for upgrade of actions, liber sophiae or kontors ? then another player will have an easy game with upgrading the 'sac' or keys, or he simply makes a chain of city's...
You have constantly be aware of what other players are doing.
This is for a large part a tactical game. 
But don't underestimate the strategical challenge ; do you prefer the city - bonus markers - Arnhem/Stendhal strategy ? this is rather slow.
Or do you like the  liber sophiae - Coellen combo ? faster but more risky...
scales very well for 3-5 players. Optimal with 3 of 5 players. Not tried with 2.
This is  essentially a race game ; upgrade as fast as possible your desired functions, place faster a merchant in Coellen or a kontor in a specific city than your opponents. 
- 
scripted opening turns ; for me not really a bad thing. In chess, the first 10 moves are usually preprogrammed.
Not really inventive mechanics , but the whole system is really better  than the sum of its parts.So who cares ?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Any new entry into the overcrowded niche of games revolving around cubes being pushed around on a map that depicts your choice of ancient egypt, medieval germany or renaissance italy has to have a pretty good excuse for its existence. Far as I can tell after two games played, Hansa Teutonica offers two things that are both fresh and, furthermore, very well implemented into the flow of the game.

First is the fact that judging the plans of your fellow players will not only allow you to hinder them, but if they should choose to overcome your hindrance, you will get a substantial reward for it (in the form of more cubes to push around, but I’ll stop the sarcasm now). This can even lead to the interesting dilemma where you’re torn between either just positioning a standard merchant cube into the path of your opponent - small hindrance, small reward - or one of your special super merchants with both bigger hindrance and reward, but the substantial risk that the opponent might forego that particular battle altogether, which means no reward at all. Nice.

Second are the three end conditions with their possibilities for prolonging or shortening the game. Whether you’d prefer one or the other probably depends on whether your strategy focuses on making quick victory points or first building your trading empire (i.e. tech up to push cubes better faster more). The two games I participated in developed in very different ways, but never felt just chaotic, and the biggest compliment for Hansa Teutonica might be that, although it is one of the most strategically open Eurogames I know, it never sprawls.

Played twice.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
[b]First impression of the game?[/b]
To be honest, I was a little disappointed in this game after the first play. The group I taught it to seemed  bewildered by some of the mechanics and the array of scoring options. My friend Corey, a notoriously strong player, homed in on the extra actions and by the time everyone started playing aggressively, he had run away with the game. This is unfortunate because he has subsequently declared the game broken—which it is not. Needless to say it took several months before I was able to get this back on the table for another try, and I am happy to report: my disappointment was unfounded. This is a great game.

[b]What are the highlights?[/b]
This is a very tactical game. In the tradition of a El Grande, you need to react to the other players and the strategies they are pursuing. The game rewards clever play and each of the items on the escritoire are vital to success. This is a game you wish had a computer implementation so you could play it over and over to explore the different strategic paths.

[b]What are the drawbacks?[/b]
The game has been described as dry and abstract, but I just don't see that. The mechanics of the game give me a feeling of the cut-throat wheeling and dealing of a merchant network. The only drawback with game could possibly be the group you play with. This is a slugfest and players have to play aggressively in order for the game to really shine.

[b]Who would enjoy this game?[/b]
People who like games like El Grande, Keythedral or LaCitta, where you have to play aggressively and think on your feet.

[b]Who would [i]not[/i] enjoy this game?[/b]
This is a gamer's game, no doubt about it. Casual gamers wouldn't get past the explanation.

[b]Would you play this game again?[/b]
Yes. I think if you had a group that played this game often, you could bang out a game in an hour as an evening closer.

This game grows on me a little bit more with each play  I may eventually buy it...

RATING: 8
One of my favourite games that I don't play very often. I do have the Britannia expansion and this is excellent for the new two player rules. I haven't got the East expansion .... yet. I figured I need to play it a bit more regularly before I can justify getting that.

The game is great. Dry but great. It is no peaceful modern euro with us all moving our pieces and maximising our efficiency and slightly perturbing each other with minor denials of resources. No this is euro engine building warfare, albeit played out by placing merchants and traders on roads. Constantly battling for positions on routes, the game rewards deliberate blocking as you always get to reposition more numerously on the board in an adjacent route if you are knocked out.

It is great. If you don't like interaction, don't worry still try it. The interaction is so constant it becomes normal (I am not sure this is always a great advert for trying something!) and hardly feels overtly aggressive at all. You are always beneficially rewarded. So when you do knock someone out, smile sweetly, apologise to the other player, point out the benefits of them relocating and then gloat in your now control of that essential route!

Definate keeper. Probably in my top 10 games.

RATING: 10
This was my favorite christmas present from my wife in 2012.  The extremely simplistic rules (once you actually get through them) combine into an incredibly deep experience.  I really recommend this for either 3 or 5 players as that's when the board is most 'full' as was intended.

EDIT: 04/04/13:
If you're thinking about buying this game then PLEASE read the FAQ and revised English rules.  We've been playing it for four months and only just realized that we were playing the Bonus Tokens completely 100% wrong.

EDIT 12/18/2013:
Having played the game a good dozen+ times now, each time the game is completely different, and strategies evolve from game to game.  There is no one winning strategy that I have found, and people usually form metas against the previous winning strategies.  I would love to get a regular group of Hansa T. players together to really explore the repeatability of the game.

RATING: 8
*Sept 2012* Finally! A pure Euro in my collection! Why doesn't the guy on the box rejoice in my feeling of completeness?

*Jan 2013* Finally! Got to play it! One game, 4 players. It's brilliant. Perfectly balanced, 0.001% luck, deep. Tons of ways to score points and they're all tugging you into different directions. This game felt almost chess-like, and some of my opponents called it an "Agricola killer" which, while the two games are very different, I can totally see. It's got the tense VP building of Gric but without the frustrations of the harvest! Excellent game....

Only issue with it is that it's not the kind of game I see on my shelf and am excited to play. Once the decision to play is made, that's when the excitement starts. More of a reflection on my self than the game, but it gets an 8 after one play. Will probably go up, game looks like it is a keeper!

RATING: 7
Tons of paths to victory in this euro with a board of Hanseatic League territories.  You play your wooden cubes and discs in houses along several paths between cities in order to fill up and claim the entire path, thereby gaining an advantage from one of the adjacent cities.  Very nice competition with three, with gamers readily dissolving into outright comparison of VP generation strategies.  Could see how the number of different VP generation ideas lends itself well to re-playability.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Thanks to a friend who requested to play this, I finally got around to giving this a try. I really liked it and am looking forward to playing with more than two players. The rules and actions make it seem extremely dry and slow but in fact it was rather dynamic and fun. The different activities come together in a nice way, not as in certain other games (looking at you, Orleans). While there may be a lot of blocking (and interaction!) there seem to be enough different useful things (skills, bonus markers, east-west connection, network) to do. I did enjoy the opportunistic style of play that arises from this, especially perhaps through the old two-player rules (only region adjacent to special pawn can be interacted with except teleportation).

Update: With five players the game dragged a little and there sometimes seemed to be too little space. Well, we were also essentially inexperienced so maybe better play can fix it.

It's a game where you really play your opponents rather than just the mechanics. Seems good.

RATING: 7
Played twice.  A super-complicated game that has a million cool things to do in it, only some of which get you points.  While I love the diversity of things to do, and the many, many paths one can take to victory, I seem to have a total mental block on how to get points in this damn game.  In my first two games I had brilliant strategies that left me about 6 turns away from scoring a few gazillion points when the game ended.

One thing I'm finding is that I don't particularly like how the game ends immediately upon one person getting 20 in-game points.  It seems relatively easy for a person to build up to getting points quickly, making all other strategies fail (with no easy way to counter it).  Until I see a game end without someone getting a ton of in-game points quickly (before, say, network strategies can take off), I'm not too enamored.

RATING: 2
This game just isn't for me. I see the quality here, but it definitely has that dry, chess-like feel to it. This is about the closest thing to multiplayer Chess, and it feels every bit as boring.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Hansa Teutonica incorporates many elements I like in boardgames. There is worker placement, where you have to beat the other players to attractive positions on the map. There is connection, where you have to build a trade network. There is area control, where you have to control cities. There is an economic engine, where you constantly have to decide which abilities to improve next. Add to that the ability to displace other players' workers that not only costs you a worker but also gives the other player an extra worker.

All this is accomplished through very few components (a map, plenty of cubes and some markers) and very swift actions (place cubes to connect and remove cubes to unlock abilities) that makes the gameplay quick and streamlined.

Hansa Teutonica has much in common with other great games like Power Grid and El Grande but in a more compressed and approachable format. A game of Hansa Teutonica is simply a delightful experience full of challenging decisions and cutthroat interaction.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
In old Europe, one is trying to establish trading posts to build offices or upgrade their skills. After getting all their markers between a city, a player may choose to occupy a city, or upgrade. Each player starts with a mat that outlines 5 skills and during the game, earns more cubes and skills as they upgrade their various abilities. There are various ways to score points, including earning bonus markers , occupying cities or connecting cities. 

This is a good game, with many tough choices to make.
Do you focus on skills? Trying to build a road net? The only drawback of this game is people can block you, fairly or unfairly, or they can displace your cubes. Sometimes being targeted can feel unfair, but it is part of the game. 

The two player game introduces a pawn so that players can only take actions where the pawn is located. It's a bit artificial to get interaction, and would not recommend the 2 player game.

After one play, I don't think I have enough of a handle on this game to assign a rating to it. RATING: 1

Seriously, put this passive-aggressive optimization exercise in a bag and throw it in a river.  I spent most of the game constantly denied the opportunity to actually DO anything, as nonsensical bidding wars for the movement track neutered me to a virtual standstill. Everyone else said what a great job I did (I was three points out of 1st place).  No one could understand why only having 2 actions for easily 75% of the game was a negative play experience.

And I think that's the problem.  Somewhere along the way to creating a robust Euro-esque exercise in partially claiming routes for Spain, sissy-fight style, someone forgot what fun looked like.  In its place is a whole bunch of middle-aged guys telling you where the best move on the board is, while they cock-block the best moves on the board for 90 straight minutes and the only remaining moves are fairly obvious to anyone who can read at a 5th-grade level, all while staring at a board that even without pieces gives me a headache.

When people talk about not enjoying Euro games, this is Exhibit A.  It's not broken; it does exactly what it says it will do.  It's 90 minutes of playing defensive billiards, which is about 75 minutes too long and saps whatever fun could be had straight into the garbage can.  I haven't hated a game this hard in a while.

RATING: 7.75
The rules are deceptively simple; but before the rules, the theme is also deceptive. When you read the manual, all the mechanics make sense in the commerce theme, but when you get to play it, the theme goes away and the game feels totally abstract. 

At the other side of its boring cover, the game is not as simple as it seems. It features many, many ways to victory. Unlike other similar games, you feel totally lost in the first game: you are not sure what to do to in order to maximize your chances at winning, and you don't have enough experience to follow what your opponents are doing. The game features heavy interaction, you will be constantly messing with each other plans and if you disregard one opponent, you might discover your mistake when its already too late.

The other most enthralling feature of this game is its lenght: ¡only 1 hour or less!

First impression [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/184429/item/3712489#item3712489]here[/url]

RATING: 8
A bit mixed feelings about the game. In general Hansa Teutonica is type of a game I usually like much but gameplay is at least as much about blocking your opponents game as it is building your own position - a thing I just don't generally like too much in games. Blocking seems mandatory part of the game because you both slow down your opponents and gain extra resources by doing so. There is also a small king maker effect because those who are being pushed around most gain extra resources from it. This whole back & forth dancing with blocking is what bothers me most about in game. All important routes seem to be in constant state of battle. 

Update (30.5.2010): I decided to increase my rating from 7 to 8 after a few more games even though I seem to have love/hate relationship with this game. Good game with 3-5 players. Haven't tried (and probably never will) 2 player game.

Update (1.11.2010): Math traded away since the game is one of those that are best when played with pretty stable group (or at least everyone knows game rather well) and there was too much hate towards the game in my regular group. (And I had personal issues too. One of the better games I've let go.)

RATING: 9

RATING: 10
Great little engine-building cube-pushing eurogame. And I say that with all fondness. Like Caylus, it has some slight randomness (in the bonus markers), but other than that, there is no luck present; just your strategy against your opponents' strategies.

I have not spent enough time analyzing to be confidence in knowing if some or most of the seemingly endless variety of strategic paths are viable or not. There's a lot you can do in this game, and you can spend your time spinning wheels or you can spend it being productive. It's hard to see which is which without giving it some thought.

Upgrading to a 10, as this game has clearly entranced me.

RATING: 9.15
[2010/02/08]

So far I've played this twice, 1st time no-one had played... second time only I had played... In both scenarios I played with gamers...

Immediately after my first game, everyone wanted to play again (we didn't, but we could have)

It's very hard to describe Hansa Teutonica... on my second play I taught the game, and I did great for teaching it... but i still wasn't able to describe it. So without describing the game to you, here is my opinion... I really reall really enjoy this game... This is only my second "newer game" I've rated above a 9... eveyone i've played with really really really enjoys this game too... there are a lot of options, and the game self regulates via "blockage" in the same manor that auctions self regulate...

the only downside I saw so far is that this game didn't encourage much player discussion or chatter.... so your not going to have as much banter as you might in other games...


Turns are exceptionally fast (or should be)... and this game rolls like butta... there can be a few move where you need to think extra hard... but not many... in most cases when it gets to be your turn you already have your turn mapped out...and if everyone does that, then you've hit the sweet spot (hense the little talking due to everyone watching everyone elses moves constantly)


HIGHLY RECOMENDED - For gamers only


I'm excited to play again... I'm hoping that this game doesn't wear out or get predictable.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
LOVED the flexibility and multiple strategies.  Gaining points from others' actions is enjoyable.  

We were giving points for each office on routes during our first two games and wondered why the game went so fast.  Caught that mistake and really enjoyed this.  Plenty of strategies to try out.

Now, we almost always take longer at games than the power gamers who post about them.  However, we have found Hansa Teutonica passes very quickly for us.  There are individual turns where someone deliberates between various good moves, but our games are taking 45-90 minutes, depending on player count.  All of the one space cities on the 2-3 player board causes that one to fill up more quickly.  And it feels short too, which is a good thing.  We get depth, variety, and strategy, but it's fast enough that we feel like playing another game afterward.  

In our games, the first few all ended because of points.  Now it's a race between points and cities, with bonus markers also a factor if we have more than 2 and someone focuses on them a little bit.

RATING: 8.5
心得:
好像要三人以上會比較好玩
遊戲節奏蠻明快的
得分路線很多元
升級也是很特別的系統 
規則:
1.當城市的格子滿時，城市token前進一格
2.一個方塊記分，起始玩家拿1圓5方(存6方)，之後順位遞增1方
3.踢方要付一個，踢圓要付兩個，被踢可一到鄰近路徑，並從存貨區拿1(2)放
4.宣告完成道路時，兩邊城市控制者(最多或最右邊)加1分，可直接拿bonus版塊，bonus版塊可以在之後的行動點使用(不能跟拿到版塊的那次行動連動)
5.該玩家行動完要補bonus版塊，只能放在空的路徑上且至少一邊有城市
6.放城市一定要從最左邊放，圖形要符合，且token必須出自完成該道路時的token，城市一有分馬上加
7.左右城市連線，第一個+7分，之後4分2分
8.結束條件 a 20分 b 要補bonus沒得補 c 10個城市完成， 條件一完成就結束，不管該玩家行動有沒有剩
9.最後記分 a到頂技能4分 b bonus版塊記分 1:1 2-3:3 4-5:6 6-7: 10 8-9:15 10+:21  c Coellen 原塊分數 d 控制的城市一個兩分 e 最長連結城市，分支都算，一個城市內方塊X分
11.交換城市位置可不管方圓，

RATING: 9
Wow! I didn't imagine I would like this game as much as I did! On its face, Hansa Teutonica looks heavy, esoteric and not very fun. But the clarity and simplicity of the actions, the swiftness of the turns and the development of different strategies, all make the game one of my favorites. The best part of the game, I've noticed, is the discussion of different strategies, post-game. For some, it's to focus on upgrading actions, for others it's about getting "displaced" by other players to allow for more options. It doesn't work like a lot of Euros; the game itself is so malleable, so open to other players' strategies whereas, normally, the strategy is one-track and too boring. Hansa, thankfully, eliminates all that in its design. Wonderful game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Very dry game of cube placement, aiming to own all the cube spaces between 2 towns so as to either earn the tech tree improvement offered by the town or place an ownership marker in the town to earn VPs. Tech tree improvements include extra actions each turn, increased cube replenishment rate, more merchants (souped up cubes), etc. There's lots of ways to score points in the game, so it's a matter of finding a strategy that doesn't have competition and being strict about it. There's not a lot of tension but there's enough pussage in the game (a la special action chits and blocking moves) to keep things interactive enough. In fact one of the issues I have with the game is that if players are too intensely focussed on playing blocking moves so as to get bonus cubes out (when the blocks are subsequently dislodged), the game can become one of constant denial and frustration at not being able to make progress. This has made one game particularly unenjoyable, enough to lower the rating and turn me off playing for a while. Anyway, with limited actions, turns go quickly enough so that downtime isn't an issue. Not sure I'd ever want to play this with more than 3 again given you want to keep it pacy. The game doesn't make me come alive due to its repetitive nature (placing cubes over and over again), but it at least works well, it provides a nice mental workout and there seems to be lots of different scoring strategies to explore.

RATING: 8
I have not come across a single person that I have introduced this game to who hasn’t liked it a lot. I think that says it all for a game with very little theme and ‘interesting’ art (some of my friends actually like it!). It is the core mechanics that make this game so good, the displacement mechanic is brilliant and leads to a lot of interesting tactical blocking, as well as bluffing so that other players help you achieve your goals.

There are also a number of different strategies that are viable and the whole tempo of the game is run by the players themselves, with three ways for the game to end all being actions that players can choose to do, or not. This game is a keeper.

RATING: 8
This game centers around a Goa-esque tech track, but instead of a once-around auction, it features route-claiming on a map.  The game is very dynamic, as pieces are constantly being added to, moved around, and removed from the board.  You never feel stuck or hemmed in as in other connection games, as you can always remove an opponent's piece from a route; however this costs you extra cubes and lets that opponent place even more cubes on the board (albeit in less advantageous positions).  I only have a few minor quibbles with this game: the "actions" track is too strong, and I wish the board gave more indication graphically of which ability was related to which route.

(5+ plays)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Very interesting game with many different strategies and ways to score points. What sets Hansa Teutonica apart from other multiple-path games, though, is its high level of player interaction. This is done by rewarding players to block each other. So you can follow a strategy over the long term, but from turn to turn you're mostly tactically reacting to other players. You're making moves and counter-moves, not only to hinder your opponents, but also to develop your actions and get more cubes on the board, which you can then use to pursue your long-term goal.

It is very open. You can do whatever; everything you do is worth points. And if you really focus on something and you aren't stopped, then you'll likely win. Of course you will be stopped, especially when your opponents catch on to what you're trying to accomplish. Hindering you also helps them. So you need to be flexible, and try to find out where the openings are, but you also have to commit at some point to score a lot of points in any one area.

I love how the rules that tell you what to do are very simple. You only have five possible actions, and they're all really generic: get cubes, place a cube on a free spot on a route, place on an occupied spot, move from one spot to another, or claim a route and remove the cubes. And you keep doing this until an end-of-game trigger. That's it. But there are so many different places to put your cubes, it feels like the possibilities are endless.

Hansa Teutonica is a great game for people who like to get a lot of plays out of a game. There's so much to explore, and which strategies are winning really depends on what the other players are doing. It also means that if you play this only occasionally, it can feel a bit all over the place.

This lack of focus stops me from rating it higher than an 7; I like tight games with clear boundaries and objectives. And I move on quickly to games that I haven't played for a long time, rather than sticking to one game for multiple plays. But if this ever gets an online version, or if my group requests this a lot, and with more plays I start getting a more complete picture of all the different strategies and everything else that's going on, I expect my rating to go up. For an occasional play it's a little too open and unbounded. Also, if players don't really know what they're doing, they can easily throw the game to someone by giving them many points or not blocking when they should.

I also don't like it with 3 (and I hear 2 is worse). The problem with three players is that in Hansa Teutonica blocking and getting displaced is extremely strong. Much stronger than doing your own thing and letting the others fight it out; you need to be involved. The defense to blocks is not taking the bait and go elsewhere, but there's one track where that's not an option: the extra action city track. It's too strong to let someone have it, so there will be a stalemate. The 'remove 3' bonus tile breaks it, which is why there'll probably also be a stalemate to get that tile. The 'opening game' will be half an hour of stalemate after stalemate without anyone getting anywhere. When the stalemates get broken, and two players get the extra action, they can easily keep the third player from ever getting it too, and the game will at that point be over for that person. So for them the game will be a frustrating opening followed by a frustrating end game without ever really getting to play.

The fix is probably really simple, though: let everyone start with the extra action. Four/five players play on a different board, where there are two tracks that give an extra action, so I doubt there will be much of a problem there.

RATING: 8.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
A route building game where the road part of the route clears once it's completed allowing multiple players to score points and claim connected rewards over the same route. Certain routes unlock better versions of your actions as you remove tokens from your board and add them to your available route tokens. When you complete a route, you have the opportunity to place a token in one of the two towns the route connected which can score you points each time it's completed after that. Not only that, but there are endgame points for controlling contiguous cities with area-control style majority control rules.

Some of the important routes in the base game map get a little cluttered and hard to complete. I highly recommend playing with the Eastern expansion map if you can, but if not, the base game is ultimately fine.

:star: Art and components
:star: Innovative mechanics
:star: Handles 5 or more players
:star: Good with min or max players
:star: Low time between turns
:star: Comfortable complexity
:star: Re-playability
:star::star::star: Enjoyment

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
European route-building game.  Nothing that particularly distinguished it for me, other than the actions mechanic.  As you progress through the game, you can upgrade a number of aspects of your play; more points for long routes, more flexibility, and more actions.  Taking more actions can not only give a player points, and the ability to do more things total than other players; it also gives them more room in terms of routes and not being blocked.  Starting out, everyone gets 2 actions.  If one player goes for actions, everyone probably should -- the words arms race come to mind.  Otherwise, one player is taking 5 actions for every 2 by everyone else; in addition they get those five actions in a row, making them much more difficult to block or interfere with in any way.

RATING: 7

I also like how development of your personal board releases resources and provides you with more flexibility, but only a few board locations allow for that development. The network on the board can't be ignored either since it scores significant points at the end of the game. The players have to be quite competitive to get items unlocked and to start the network construction.

RATING: N/A
I frequently refer to this as "the best game I've gotten rid of," but I wonder if that's because I have so badly whiffed on a lot of purchases, despite initial enthusiasm and that "this game is [i]really[/i] going to work for my group, just you wait and see" vibe that one gets after spending too much time without a functioning filter in the recommendation forums here at BGG (where everyone eventually gets Lords of Waterdeep as a recommendation). 

Hansa Teutonica has a shared map for some semblance of network building (albeit not permanent, as you remove your presence from the board once you've finished a route), though you're mainly trying to improve several different personal abilities on your player board, which is a sort of...Merchant Ability Tracker? It's highly abstracted, and that counts as a virtue in my world. You can interfere with other players, you can block them. It's not fully a "diddle with your own player board" Euro. But it is still partially a Board Diddler. 

It was very dry, the official 2p variant was awful, and the longer the game goes, the more the player paths diverge until the end sees everyone doing their own thing, cranking on their own personal strength(s), and tallying points. I think this got the interest that it did because interaction has become almost wholly absent from the Eurogameworld, and any little flicker of it causes people to get tingly with glee.

RATING: 3
Nov 2017: Played this again with a bunch of Wallace fans. The game totally bombed despite the fact of all players totally understanding all the game's concepts. I think it's because there's a thematic and visual contradiction between what the game looks like (a map with routes connecting cities) and what you're actually doing with your cubes.

Sep 2017: This game rubbed me the wrong way. I think it's because of this: it looks like a network building game, but it's actually a worker placement game. 

It's clever that it reduces workers, contract and building cards, resources, and money to only cubes and a separate player boards to keep track of engines.

At least there are not too many rules or special power cards.

Much, much lighter than I expected. And way, way more tactical than expected as well. I thought players would scatter their cubes all over the board, almost like Go. Instead, players focus on the special powers, then once their engines are up and running, they start spreading across the board.

RATING: 10
Luckily, the person who explained the rules to Hansa Teutonica is very good at explaining rules or I would have been utterly lost.  That said, I was not very good at Hansa Teutonica.  Sometimes, when I am horrible at a game it chases me away and I do not want to play.  More often though, my lack of skill only fuels my desire to play a game again.  That is certainly the case with Hansa Teutonica.  It's got many aspects I really enjoy in a game.  First, it has a ton of different paths to victory.  There are so many that it is really overwhelming at first.  I am sure tha I didn't remember all of the different ways to score points during my first play.  It is important to interfere with the other players' strategies while having your own defined strategy.  Hansa Teutonica is a tightrope of a game with almost limitless possibilities. 

***Update***

I raised my rating when I realized how similar Hansa Teutonica is to one of my other favorite games: Notre Dame.  Like Notre Dame, there are almost limitless possibilities for strategies.  Also, what other players do affects which strategies will be the most effective.  I've been searching for a game that scratches the same itch as Notre Dame and I've found it in Hansa Teutonica.  For that reason, I give Hansa Teutonica a nine!

***Update***

I raised my rating to a ten.  I play this game all the time.  It's fantastic.

RATING: 9.7
Upgraded my rating from a mid-8 to 9.7.

Yes, it is JASE(just another souless Euro). So what? How much "soul" does some heavily-themed, kill-or-be-killed Ameritrash epic have without real blood and the smell of singed flesh??

All theme is vanity. Vanity!!

The questions:

1) Does it make you think?

2) Do you have to react to others?

3) Is it any damn fun??

Hansa T. is all that to me.  It requres the utmost of my concentration on the subject at hand, and yet, I must respond to my opponents' subtle strategies.

RATING: 10
It's hard to imagine anyone doing a better area-control game than this. For me, Bruxelles 1893 is (possibly) as good and I think both 'scratch a very similar itch'.

It is a really clever game with a short-ish rulebook and few components - but a lot of depth. It is wonderfully un-fiddly, 'immediate' and very interactive.

Essentially you are just putting your cubes and discs down onto the game map in ways that delay other players and set up scoring opportunities, including establishing permanent little emplacements called 'offices'. At the same time you are expanding your options and refining your strategy by levelling-up skills on your player board.

Multiple 'game end' conditions mean players can accelerate or delay when it ends, which creates exciting finales. The minimal luck element creates a strong feeling of achievement when you are doing well.

Despite the dry theme, it is actually very cutthroat. It feels more like you are airlifting troops, laying minefields and building forts ... which is good.

It has a (good) habit of fooling the winner into thinking they've unlocked a 'winning formula', only for them to find it doesn't work on the next game. Players adapt and the 'metagame' evolves constantly.

You are rewarded strongly for blocking other players with your pieces, leading to constant "I CAN'T BELIEVE YOU DID THAT TO ME!" moments. Everyone I know has found this fun, not frustrating, as revenge is swift and easy.

There is huge freedom of movement on the board and you can rapidly switch from one play style to another. As the game progresses it becomes more obvious what each player's 'winning strategy' will be, then there are great decisions to be made about how much you block them or push yourself forward.

There can be temporary alliances too - although usually only towards the end, when players try to keep whoever is ahead under control. I'm pleased that it doesn't have any overwhelming catch-up mechanisms. A player can always find useful things to do, even if everyone wants to stop them.

I also love how the entire game fits into its small box (by modern standards) - including its two expansion maps and mini expansion (as of now, March 2017). We tried the East Expansion recently and loved it.

RATING: 8
(Note: just started rating, so my rating may change.... it MAY go to a 9. I doubt any further down. And the expansion map may just replace it in the 10 spot).

This game is amazing. Scales well for 3-5 (at least). Tech trees, all of them viable. Multiple end conditions (well, probably only two). Strategy and tactics. A small random element which is unlikely to hose a player, but will add enough variance to ensure games play differently, even if people play with static strategies. Which they probably won't... I'm still learning things about the game. The ability to interfere with any players strategy, but at the same time never stop them completely... and they always have the ability to switch gears and punish you for interfering. I could sit down and play this game all day.

I am, however, slowly starting to wonder if player order is relevant... It certainly isn't Puerto Rico relevant, but it may have more relevance then I though originally. This probably won't be much of an issue unless you're playing with people who have a large skill variance. And it [i]could[/i] lead to AP - people could constantly be counting final game scores, since they are computable. Don't play with those people :). Turns should be mostly quick - you have a most 5 actions, each taking no more then 3 seconds to execute, and while occasionally you'll need to think about altering your plans, you normally can figure your turn out before your turn.

RATING: 8.5
This is a really interesting route building game. You are gaining bonus abilities by going to certain cities and allowing you to do more and more as the game progresses. The real key to the game seems to be in getting into someone's way and forcing them to bump you. This forces them to use extra available pieces and also allows you to place the bumped piece along with 1 or 2 extra pieces from your supply. That allows you to get those pieces to the board without taking an action to pull them into your active pile first. Admittingly, we didn't get to finish this game, but we played enough to get a good feel for how it plays. I'll have more thoughts once I get to the end of the game and see how the final scoring works out.

(+) The game flows strikingly fluent which keeps all players involved. There is no downtime whatsoever.

Playtime: 30 min / player

RATING: 7.5
I enjoy the number of options available on each turn. In most Euros, on any given turn, players perform only one of three or four possible actions. In Hansa Teutonica, players may execute multiple TYPES of actions on a turn, with multiple options within each of those types of actions.

The "decision space" (as I've heard it called) is much larger than that of a typical euro.

Hansa Teutonica is also more interactive. Without summarizing the whole game, a player can engineer situations in which his opponents can actually help him win.

RATING: 10
Probably my favorite abstract Euro - it's a lot like a Feld game, but not Feld.  Once you get the hang of it, turns go by really fast and it doesn't seem as AP prone as others.  I really like how you are building your own tech tree but there's also lots of player interaction in competing for routes and offices.  It's not really area control, but it similar enough to El Grande and I would always prefer to play Hansa Teutonica. The main decision is whether you invest early in your tech tree or securing office spaces which are in limited supply and continue to generate points off other players.   My only minor complaint is the routes next to the extra action city seem the strongest, but that might just be groupthink.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Visual Appeal: (4)
This game doesn't really look like anything special from the outside. Just another boring Euro about merchants or some bunk. The board is nice. Where the design shows through best is in the player action boards. The design of the boards makes it easy to understand how upgrading each type of action benefits you by both increasing your resource pool and augmenting your actions. 

Components: (4)
Nice wooden euro cubes and disks. I wish the player action mats were a thicker card board. They bend, but they haven't taken any serious wear yet. There is plenty of room in the box for an expansion board as well.

Theme: (3)
Like most worker placement euro games, the theme is fairly dry. I am not likely to get many people excited about the Hanseatic League. However, the actions are fairly in line with the idea of controlling trade in certain cities and paying resources to block competitors from your desired areas.

Learning: (2)
The rule book for this game is not a particularly good one. That is unfortunate because it makes a fairly simple game seem really complicated. I hope many people were not scared away from this game because of that. I have made my own player aides and found a method of teaching that works for me, but the rule book is too wordy and unclear.

Gameplay: (5)
It is so tricky to balance blocking opponents and going for what you need with the risk of getting blocked or booted out. There are a lot of approaches to take: you can try to upgrade your actions board first, or go for a large network of trading houses. Whichever you choose, other players can always interfere in the hopes of getting the upper hand in positioning and resources. Either way, the clock is ticking. There is a point cap that triggers in game scoring. Early trading houses in contested areas can drastically shorten games.

Overall Fun: (5)
The more the merrier with this game. There is enough to think about on your opponents' turns that you will likely not get bored. I don't particularly like the 2 player variant. It is truly a 3-5 player game, and my #1 favorite euro.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Another cube-pusher where the placement and movement of cubes is governed by a language of rules you just have to be versed with. But setup time is minimal, and the rules are very original, infusing your regular tired Euro with a heavy dose of board-play. This game conveys a feel of 'dominating the board' in a truer way than traditional territorial conquest games. It's also a maverick outta nowhere that didn't cleave to the general direction game development was heading, and found interesting nuances in things that seemingly don't add up to a game, yet do, like a Power Grid or a Tigris. It is still original, though the emulation is coming in other games where you peel cubes of your player boards to acquire skills e.g. Eclipse, Terra Mystica.

Try to connect cities (through a dance of placing one at a time, moving several at once and paying through the nose to displace blockers to somewhere they probably wanted anyway), and collect benefits either as upgrades that give more cubes/discs among others, or owning the route to collect VPs from all future claimants but also give up a cube/disc permanently. Also plan out your network and time the game end (first to 20 VPs).

A bit leery that it's just optimization 101 on your turn, and grabbing whatever chance just opened up.

4+3
