RATING: 9.5
[COLOR=#663300][b][size=11]Hansa Teutonica[/size][/b][/COLOR]
[COLOR=#663300][i]Note: My numeric ratings disregard BGG's associated verbal descriptions.[/i][/COLOR]

[COLOR=#006600][b]Comments: [/b][/COLOR] 

With the exception of fillers, I almost always prefer to follow the play of one game with a different game.  (Yes, I'll play [thing=5]Acquire[/thing] two or three times in a row — but generally I prefer to switch games.)

Hansa Teutonica is the first game in some time that had me wanting to play again on the same day.  A nice balance of strategy and tactics which is deep enough to satisfy, but quick enough to feel light.  Yes, the beginning can feel scripted, since increasing one's available actions is crucial, but interests will branch quickly enough to allow actual choice into the mix.  And the choices are limited enough to keep play moving, but meaningful enough to impact subsequent gameflow.

Sadly, turn order is rather too important.  Reaching three actions quickly is important, and players starting later are at a disadvantage as regards gaining that third action.  Their additional playing pieces do provide some balance, but falling behind the action-curve can be bothersome.  This was particularly noticeable in the five-player game.

Jesse Dean (doubtofbuddha) really nails it with his comments:
[COLOR=#666666]"What makes it stand out for me is the elegant interactions that game allows between players, as players are constantly competing for technologies, kontors, and game length. Individual players are incentivized to claim routes, while other players clearly would like to stop them. Rather than making this a zero sum affair, the designer implemented a genius action point system, where removing a player simply costs the displacing player some of their action points in exchange for giving the displaced player additional board presence, and a potential reduction in the number of actions required to claim a particular route somewhere else on the board.

"Because of this, and other similar mechanics, there are very few zero sum aggressive actions in the game, making it so there is always a question of whether displacing an opponent is more helpful to the displacing player or the displaced player. Is getting this increased technology worth giving your opponent additional points and giving them greater control over the game length? Did your opponent place that piece because they wanted to work towards claiming that route, or is it simply a distraction as they prepare for claiming another route in one turn?"[/COLOR]

2nd Edition Changes: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/article/5106380#5106380

RATING: 1.5
I don't hate it. The best part of the game is spacial element and another which is interesting is needing a few plays to be able to evaluate the map. And it's interesting how your strategy of engine building reflects itself on the map. Interactiveness is quite good for this level - leeching, subtle blocking, actual blocking. 

Problems for me are: I don't find engine building of this type that exciting - a sort of tech tree actually narrows down strategic paths instead of opening the space wide.  The cubage in the game is just ridiculous. The theme part doesn't strike me as important as theme for me matters in terms of connection of gameplay to theme. Here this problem isn't present as there's no theme - this is an abstract game. (as opposed to thin theme) I found the graphics of the board pleasing for an abstract. I'll take T&E any day over this, also Torres, it's on the level of El Grande (and will probably drop to that rating eventually). 

In short: best thing - spacial elements on the map. Worst thing - "tech" tree. I don't get (euro) gamers euphoria over tech trees, plus this one is not really tech, just building up your actions. Where the spacial elements build the game between players, this "tech tree" is a needlessly fiddly solo puzzle.

RATING: 7
Played two 5P games. I have a semi-interesting history with Hansa Teutonica. The fact that there was a 4-year gap between my first and second game notwithstanding, I somehow managed a remarkable score of zero in my very first game... 

Anyway, at the core of Hansa Teutonica, you want to complete routes with your traders, but early game limitations enable your opponents to drop their traders into your turf. These meddlesome traders can be displaced at a cost, and often to the benefit of opponents.

What's interesting is that as the game progresses, the incentives to either stay out of, or interfere with your opponents' plans intensify. Advancing on the tech tree allows you to complete your routes more efficiently in a single turn, but getting traders into cities also becomes increasingly important. The concept of placement and displacement is executed well here. Displaced traders allow their owner to place more traders, so players are likely to interfere with their opponents purely out of spite. This opens up plenty of tactical considerations without being too unbalanced. The rudimentary tech tree and bonus tiles then opens up different strategic possibilities in scoring points.

If there are any complaints about this title, it's because the opening is fairly scripted – going for additional actions seems to be a necessity early in the game. Also, the subject matter is dry and presented in an abstract manner at best.

RATING: 10
[18 plays] This is also a great 2 player game.

I love that there are no cards, no dice, and yet so much going on with basically just cubes and a map.

Update Oct 2014: This is a top 10 all time game for me. So much complexity and depth, yet the moves on any single turn can usually be completed fairly quickly by all players. After 12 plays, I would still say that we are only beginning to scratch the surface of what it takes to play a great game of HT and react well to the routes the other players at the table are taking. LOVE IT!

Update March 2015: The Brittania map is delightful. I can't see playing it any other way for a long time, unless maybe teaching someone new the base game. This is truly a masterpiece of game design. Like chess, the moves and the pieces are limited, but visioning the board several turns ahead and reacting well to what your opponents are trying to do is the difference between reacting to others and forcing them to react to you. 

Most recent play Dec '15.

RATING: 7.5
A nice light little euro.  A fun little extortion game.  Lay some cubes down, pick them up, position yourself in key spots and upgrade your power.  It's cute, but seriously, this game is only about laying cubes down in a line and then picking them back up again.

I fear that my experience will be too clouded by group think to allow for the full range that the game might have.  It seems that the Action power is vastly more important than the other powers.  So there is a constant hotbed of activity at the bottom of the board to get Actions, with the other roads connected to super powers getting some regular decent play and the center getting near nothing unless there is a super power chit that people want.  

Now I know if you are able to make the big connect you can end the game before others can use all their fancy shmancy powers, but, I haven't seen it happen yet...

I haven't played the game enough to say this for sure, but my suspicions are that this game would be far more interesting if the Actions were nerfed dramatically.

RATING: 10
I believe Hansa Teutonica to be one of the best games ever made. 

One of the most impressive parts of this game are its tiny amount of components: just the board, the cubes, and some discs, and thats pretty much it. All the wonderful complexity of this game comes from the elegant design itself. Of course, i love games with tons of bits too (what geek doesn't?) but it is so nice to sometimes just open a box, place a small amount of pieces out and bam - start playing. I also respect the minimalist approach from a design standpoint. 

So, what is so amazing about this game, with its little luck, the most overdone theme in all boardgamedom, and seemingly dry gameplay? Tom Vassal said it best when he mentioned that even the gentleman on the box cover looks miserable. So, what is it?? 

Well, in my opinion, this game has all the elements of a great game, but none of the frustrations present in many. The magic here is that in Hansa Teutonica, every action one takes is an excrutiating choice, which is delicious, but, but! Each choice is also a great one. Here, there is none of that familiar feeling of being backed into a corner by one's foe, and having to choose between evils. In Hansa, there is always something great to choose from - it just gives one a fantastic, perhaps subliminal feeling that you are having fun the entire time. Even when one gets jacked by another player (getting your cube bumped from a road) it comes with a benefit for you and a punishment for the them.

Other tasty aspects in this game are common to many great games: tons of ways to win with no one particular strategy reigning supreme, player interaction, very suitable play time, elegant design, tactics and strategy, etc. 

Some have complained about the ugly and unreadable graphic design of the board. I'm not sure if I have a different board than others, but i love mine. Honestly, ive thought it would be cool to print the board on a poster and hang it on the wall - it looks like some sweet medieval artwork to me.

RATING: 5
It's hard to imagine an appealing elevator pitch for Hansa Teutonica. By all appearances, it's an abstract overly complex cube-pushing euro, distinguished, if at all, by the fact that it's not the solitaire worker placement game you might expect it to be from the generic box, theme, and components. Actually, though, it's uniquely and deeply interactive. The game is all about getting other players to improve your board position for you. There seem to be multiple viable strategies, none too deeply baked in, and it's not obvious which to pursue.

Unfortunately, there's zero narrative and only the flimsiest pretext of a theme. In a game as complex as this one, that's a problem. I like to have some sense that the parts of a game amount to something, that there's life to the game beyond the math; here, instead, I'm left with the sense that Hansa Teutonica will only ever deliver precisely the sum of its parts and not an iota more.

RATING: 6
An interesting area-majority game where the area majorities are used not only to score VP, but to improve the quality of your abilities (e.g., take more actions, resupply more cubes, etc.). Seems well-balanced, except it depends on players also keeping each other in check: if someone is allowed to jump way ahead on a track (e.g., action track, resupply track) without much competition, or to set themselves up to be displaced by multiple players (and get more cubes on the board quickly), it seems they will have a very significant advantage. 

--- Summary ---
Board shows a bunch of cities connected by routes length 2-4. Each city has 1-4 spaces for tokens (cubes or discs, 4 different colors). 6 Cities also have a banner adjacent to them, corresponding to end-game VPs and the 5 different "powers" (#actions, resupply, privilege of placing in colored spaces of cities, #tokens moved per action, multiplier for end-of-game scoring). Players start with a disc and (3+turn order) cubes in Personal supply, and a personal display full of cubes/discs on several tracks (the 5 "powers"). 

On your turn, take as many actions as shown on your Action Track (2 at start of game; improved to 3/3/4/4/5). Actions are:
- place a token on an empty space on a road
- displace another player's token from a road; return 1 (or 2) cubes to the General Supply if displacing a cube (or disc). That player gets to place the displaced token, plus 1 (or 2) cube taken from General Supply, on a road adjacent to their original location.
- move 2-5 of your tokens on the board
- resupply: take x cubes from General supply to Personal supply (x starts at 3; can be improved to 5/7/all)
- claim a road: only if all spaces are occupied by your tokens. Player(s) controlling adjacent cities (i.e., majority presence; if tied, rightmost token) score 1 VP. If the road had a special token pointed at it, take it; also draw a random token face down, to be placed back on the board face up (pointing at an empty road with no other bonus marker, and with at least one empty space in an adjacent city) at end of your turn. Either place one of the tokens from the road into an adjacent city (if space available, and you have privilege to place into that color space; must use left-most empty space; some spaces give you 1VP immediately) or, if it's one of those 5 cities, improve one of your "powers" by removing the leftmost cube/disc from that track of your personal display (token goes to your Personal supply). Unused cubes go to General supply. 
- May also use any previously acquired special tokens; keep used tokens for end-game scoring. These tokens may provide extra actions for the turn, allow you to place a token in an imaginary space to the left of a city with tokens already present or swap 2 tokens' positions in a city (to give you presence and/or change majority), or let you remove other players' tokens from the board. Using these tokens does not count as one of your actions.
- First player to create a connection (via presence in cities) between marked cities in east and west scores 7 VP (4/2 for 2nd/3rd player to do so).

Game ends instantly when either 10 cities completely filled with tokens, a player gets to 20VP, or someone needs to place a special token but none available. 

End-game Scoring
- for 1 + (# special tokens acquired / 2; round down), score 1/3/6/10/15/21 VP
- 2 VP per city controlled
- (# of your tokens in city-spaces in largest contiguous group of cities where you have presence) x (end-game multiplier; the Key track) VP

Most VP wins!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
This is an action selection/route building Euro.  Players take turns selecting an action, each of which typically just pushes cubes from one location to another, in order to build routes between locations and score points.

There is a decent amount of interaction in the game as players can block other's routes and to replace an opponent's cube on the board is not only costly but gives the opponent another benefit.  It is a thinker and it is dry and I resisted playing it for so long myself because of the incredibly drab and boring looking board and components.  However, if you like games about optimized strategic planning then this one is for you.

There is some merit to the claim you can try different paths to victory since players are certainly able to affect one another.  However, because of the static nature and lack of variance in the game I fear the game can be solved and almost always the more experienced player will win this game against newer ones.

Also, a game like this is one that can be kind of ruined by players not taking up arms together against another who is running away with it.  In my first play one player (who was the most experienced) was allowed to complete the entire key track for a whopping 4 points per connected city.  Both players needed to intervene to prevent that from happening but too often players either don't recognize this or don't want to divert from their own strategy and that makes a game like this kind of fall apart.

RATING: N/A
It's a pure euro game. This isn't exactly a bad thing to be it's just that after all the hype I've heard I thought this was going to be a different game somehow. Not sure how different but different. Then again, this has nothing to do with the game itself which is very interesting with lots of clever available strategies.

Hansa Teutonica reminds me of Goa because of the improvement skills chart. Players chose to improve their abilities instead of scoring victory points in order to do better from turn to turn - have more actions, move more pieces... This decision of upgrading skills as opposed to actually score VP's is one of the most interesting things that this game has to offer. Also the skills players have at their disposal are all very valid and good to have so it turns decisions even more tense.

I really have to play this game again just to make sure if I find it average or better than that. For now it's a 7.

Components are generally average but the artwork of the board is awful. I like the box cover but not the board. I also don't like the way English rules are written.

RATING: 4
The problem with this one is not that it is a hodgepodge of existing game elements, although it is, the problem is that it is a hodge podge of extremely tactical elements that suffer from extreme player-driven chaos.  Every turn requires that you potentially ditch your previous turn's plan to deal with your opponent's current turn.

It's amusing that people call this deep since by its tactical, non-planning nature, it is anything but deep.  It's a matter of optimizing your play each turn based on an almost vacuum-state of current information.  What you do does have a downstream effect, but it does so in a way that cannot be planned for, even superficially, by the players, even a turn prior.

I think this type of non-thinky play is currently in vogue however, based on how well received 7 Wonders and Glen More are.  Though I think both of those do the same style of gameplay in a more focused and streamlined fashion which makes them more playable.

I suppose, at least, it's not completely random on top of it. so at least your current-turn tactics actually do something.

RATING: 9
Dry as dust game of something something Hanseatic League something, but a magnificent example of cube-pushing done right. It's highly interactive by eurogame standards, has multiple paths to victory, and many nice touches like control of the game clock.

I also love the artwork and don't understand complaints that it's ugly or bland.

I'm less enthused about the turn-order binding (it's very easy for an inexperienced player to give the player to their left a series of wonderful gifts) and, again especially with inexperienced players, a lack of care about giving points to players who control privilege cities. Several times I have seen a player establish an early office in the action-point city and rack up points there, with other players saying, "I need an action and it's just one point" -- individually true, but an aggregate problem. These problems make me disinclined to play the game with anyone new.

Still, it's a fantastic game and one of my all-time favourites.

RATING: 8
When I first heard of this it didn't really excite me: busy artwork; place cubes to score [i](zzzzz)[/i], but after playing it I'm kicking myself for not trying and buying it at Essen. It's fast, and clever. Limited actions means little downtime (generally). Placing cubes on the board is to satisfy small objectives (join two cities), at which point your cubes come off the board and you get the reward which is progress on some track (so you get more actions, or your actions are more efficient, or your end-game scoring will improve), or an office in a city (which will earn you VP). Lots of ways to earn VP. lots of interaction, and an interesting distinction between immediate VP and end-game VP.

Even the artwork isn't as bad up close as I feared.

RATING: 8
Solid route-building Euro. Players are set the objective of placing pieces on roads between cities, which in turn either allows them to control offices in those cities thereby earning victory points whenever someone else completes an adjoining road, or in certain cities bestows an upgrade such as gaining more actions, more markers to place on roads/cities, the right to control a greater number of offices etc. Points are tight and they key to success is gaining control of offices in cities that are likely to see a lot of action on their adjoining roads. Whilst this is certainly a Eurogame in every way, it manages to implement its theme well and their are a lot of decision to be made each turn with quite a lot of player interaction, which certainly commends it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A wonderful game about rival clans of wizards fighting for control of the ley lines through a fantasy realm. 

In this game you can make your conclave stronger by extorting control over the ley lines. Different key villages around the kingdom will provide you with different upgrades. Expending influence over a ley line leading to certain central villages will give you better teleporting spells, while control over a ley line to another village will provide you with more arch-wizards, which are harder for rival wizard clans to force off your ley lines or provide better control over villages. 

There are many ways to score points as well. If your wizards have control of the largest tower in a village next to a ley line when completed then your conclave scores a point. If you are able to own a tower along the ley lines between the two main wizard academies then you can score a lot of points.

The game ends when a conclave reaches twenty points or when all of the towers in ten villages are completed.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I am on the fence about Hansa Teutonica. I honestly don't know what to make of it. Mechanically, it's fiddly, with lots of interconnected ways to score points and collect bonuses. A strong theme would be perfect for game like this; however, the theme of the game (something I don't usually mention) actually detracted from my understanding  gameplay.

At it's heart, Hansa Teutonica combines area control (disguised as route building) with a bit of an economic snowball. There *is* route building in the game, but it has more to do with a scoring opportunity for most connected cities than the mechanic for claiming bonuses and cities. The core mechanic is sound, but the problem arises when you look at the board. Much of the fighting is going to come in the bottom half of the map as players fight for extra actions. The rest of the board becomes more interesting in the latter half of the game, sure, but by that time the fights are less interesting because everyone has more actions. More actions means they can claim a route in a single turn without much resistance -- especially if they plan out their turn properly.

It's not a bad game. By the end, I finally understood what I was doing and how all the different parts worked together. Yet I still felt like the game was a bit of a mess. Streamlining would help, perhaps. A better effort at graphic and component design would certainly help. Most of all, and surprisingly to me, the theme needs some re-working.

1/15/12 - After tonight's session, I believe my long quest to get somebody in my family hooked; and it couldn't have been a better person: MY WIFE!

RATING: 9
I was completely wrong about this game. In my GeekBuzz Meets Fairplay column, I dismissed Hansa Teutonica out of hand as appearing to be a dry and bland game. The rules and components are underwhelming, but it turns out the game itself is surprisingly good. I have played 9 times now (at all player counts from 2 to 5) and Hansa Teutonica is solidly my surprise hit of BGG.CON (just as Planet Steam was my surprise hit of BGG.CON in 2008). It's an addictive game. I find myself always wanting to play again so I can try a different strategy or try to refine the same strategy to get it to work. You have so much control over the way the game plays out from beginning to end that you feel as if you should be able to accomplish what you set out to accomplish. And yet there are so many different ways to score points that it's easy to get distracted by a short-term opportunity, at the expense of your long-term objectives. The game has a very nice flow to it as the turns move quickly, with each player only adding a few grains of sand to the eventual beach of the game on any given turn. The most remarkable thing about Hansa Teutonica is how much interaction there. It's a German-style game with lots of wooden cubes and an overused theme, but multi-player solitaire this is not. It's an in your face, almost viciously contentious battle for control of the various trade routes and city guilds across the board. Nonetheless, the game's displacement mechanism is reminiscent yet superior to that of Endeavor, as it doesn't punish the defender for getting in the way, but rather rewards them and punishes the attacker, while somehow still retaining a significant incentive to attack. I'd also be remiss not to mention the fantastic use of a technology tree in this game, which provides a delicious tension between developing each skill a little bit for flexibility and control over your board position, versus developing fewer skills more fully for bonus points. All that being said, this game is bound to disappoint many because expectations are being raised too high in the time it will take for a widespread reprint to become available. I, and many others at BGG.CON, were pleasantly surprised by Hansa Teutonica because there were no expectations. Now that the game is being talked up by so many people, I don't think it will impress people in the same way that I found myself impressed and caught off guard. Hopefully people can enjoy it for what it is and not set the bar too high in the intervening months, but knowing how much I tend to anticipate unavailable games and how often they disappoint, and having seen the impact of delays on countless prior releases, I have significant trepidation over how well Hansa Teutonica will be received by the wider market.

Purchased: November 22, 2009 (at BGG.CON)

RATING: 8.5
This game is awfully good, but it usually takes a few plays before it will play correctly.  Often, if you are playing with new players, the strategy just comes down to making use of what is ignored by the other players.  

This is a game with lots of open choices.  Everywhere you look, you see options, and it can be rather tough, especially for a new player to prioritize how to proceed.  

Based on the strategies taken by all players, you have to determine how long you think the game will go, and then tailor your strategy to work for a game of that length.  I've seen many games that abruptly end when players didn't expect it to do so.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Boring title, boring art, boring board, boring components, paper thin theme, but the gameplay is good enough to save it.  Yes, this is quite neat.  The improvements system is very similar to that of [gameid=9216], and is used very well in this game.  But the main mechanic of this game is in using your cubes to claim routes, and it sounds almost too simple when you're explaining it.  But, what really makes it stand out from most Euros is that you can mess with people, and it will still improve your game by more than just tearing down their strategy.  When somebody displaces your cubes, you get to take a cube from your out-of-play pile, and along the the cube that got displaced, you may place them both on adjacent routes.  It's so blindingly simple, but so critical to separating this game from every other euro.  I look forward to playing it further, to see if the charm holds up.

RATING: 8
What at first appear to be a bunch of random mechanics and scoring conditions thrown together with repeat plays unveil themselves to fit into a cohesive, masterful whole. Hansa Teutonica impresses me in that it crams a deep and variable game into an hour or so playing time. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
There's a lot in this game that works toward its favor with me: It moves quickly, has a variety of strategies, has very little luck, and rewards good decision making.  It's also not an overly complex game for its depth, so it might even be a good second-step gateway game.  The tech trees are integrated well into the game, and there don't seem to be any obvious strategies to take when approaching the game.  In fact, the more times I play the game, the more I discover different ways to play the game, and they all seem to be well balanced.  Each time I play with a different group, I see a different way to play the game.  That makes the game easily susceptible to groupthink, but the options are varied if you can see them when you play.  I had high hopes for this game, and it didn't let me down.

3 plays: 3 players
8 plays: 4 players
10 plays: 5 players

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
The players act as traders trying to get victory points for building a network of offices, controlling cities, collecting bonus markers or for other traders using the cities they control. After controlling a line between two cities with your pawns you can decide to build an office (and maybe also establish control and/or get a bonus marker) or to get a skill improvement from some of the cities.
\13\\13\
Players have to improve their traders' "skills" for the following effects: getting more VP from offices in their network, getting more available action points, increasing the number of available pawns, and getting the right to place pawns and get more special pawns.
\13\\13\
This game appeared originally as Wettstreit der Händler at the Hippodice competition.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
It's not very often that I find myself drastically outside the ratings norm for a game here on BGG. For the most part, I like the top 200 or so and if the heavy Euro crowd is into a game, it's usually a sure bet that I will be into it as well. Then I met Hansa Teutonica.

Now I'm not sure exactly why I can't get into it. It has a player mat with abilities that unlock as you place your pieces, tactical goals that you can dance around to improve your score and guide your decisions and a primary goal of area control, which is a mechanic I generally like. Terra Mystica features all three of those things and I absolutely adore it. Strangely, those very same features all fall flat for me here.

I don't know if it's that I'm just not into the tug of war thing, placing a guy, then bumping a guy and then having the favor returned as the other players take their turn. I really can't figure it out. I should enjoy the hell out of this, but I just find it so incredibly boring.

I plan on giving it a few more tries when the opportunity presents itself, because I must be missing something, somewhere. Of course, I have very little reason to actually want to buy it, so that may be easier said than done.

RATING: 10
Wow.  I normally don't rate games I haven't purchased but after playing this a bit I began to realize the balance is excellent and the possibilities to score are high.  This is definitely a gamer's game with no luck involved.  The decisions are yours to make and you can change strategy to come back from a bad choice or bad position.  This one has got to be the sleeper of the year for hard core Euros.  Be mindful that the game probably isn't for everyone.  If you're looking for lighter fare or a family game, look elsewhere.  This one can be a brain burner.  There's plenty of ways to get points during the game and upon completion but the actions flow quick and the game can be played out in under 1.5 hours with five experienced players.  With more exposure I think this could be a top ten game on BGG.  Well, this line is now an edit as I found a copy at Funagain and am now a proud owner.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Really clever design, Hansa Teutonica feels unique and unlike anything else. It's a very competitive and "sneaky" area control game with multiple paths to victory. I love the whole system of developing your player board and unlocking stronger actions. You can't win this game "by accident" - you really have to plan ahead and make strategic choices. Hansa Teutonica simply rewards well thought-out playing. 

That said, I have some issues with the game. The opening move feels really scripted - getting that 3rd action is a natural thing to do, so everyone will fight for it. For this reason the start of every game is pretty much the same (the game could use a little bit more variability on the board during the setup). Also, don't bother playing this game with only 2 players, it just doesn't work properly (it's best with 3-4, with 5 it gets a bit too chaotic). Still, despite all those cons I highly recommend it, there's no other game quite like it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
What I love-
The desk-like player-board that as you upgrade, give you more options and viable strategies. Multiple strategies to win make the game fun to explore the many different ways to score points. The blocking mechanism. The way I can get more pieces on the board by blocking the good routes, then use the Book action to move multiple pieces around the board... It's a strategy that most new players fail to see and may dislike it for that reason... Keep playing! My 12-yr old twins love this game.

What I Dislike-
You can definitely be out maneuvered by a skilled player.( but that's part of the game.) game can end earlier than you wish if a player rushes claiming the 'improvement routes' leading to small networks, (but again, it's one of the strategies; I just prefer maxing everything out, but in this game, you need to stay focused or the game will end when you aren't ready. 

After 10+ plays
The most amazing game design ever. Strategic as well as tactical. The most interactive Euro you are likely to play. This game gets better with every play. I cannot say that about any other game I've played. The Britannia expansion adds a whole new layer of strategies and also brings the other game end trigger to the forefront that is rarely reached in the first game, the full cities ending. Loving it!!!
My new Number 1 Game!!!!

RATING: 8
A very good game. A solid 8 if not possibly higher. A great deal of complexity. There are many changing options towards victory. It is not an easy game to teach or learn at first, because of all that is going on. It is good to learn it from an experienced player. However, once learned the game is quite easy. 

The one issue with the game that is of constant concern is the issue where you place your cubes when they come off the board. One must keep track that all players place the cubes that come off the board into their pools rather than with their available cubes. This is a recurrent mistake which is not  intentional, but easy to do.

RATING: 8
This is a fun game, it feels more like a mental duel, or a chess match, than it's supposed theme (putting traders on roads and completing routes) This isn't a bad thing, it is a ton of fun and very thinky, there is also a nice element of Chicken to it - Who's going to bump who from the road first? Or Who's going to pull their forces out and attack elsewhere? (Wait, now it sounds like a war game...)

If I had to re-theme this, it would become a Swords and Sorcery game.
Traders/Merchants == Swordsmen and Knights
The Move Action == Magical Teleportation
Upgrading your Desk == Completing Quests
... I could go on and on.

RATING: 7
You know a game is serious about multiple paths to victory when there are no less than three different ending conditions.  Hansa Teutonica is a surprising title that came out of nowhere which feature an incredible amount of strategy and player interaction.  There is a lot to consider in every move, whether it be in the placement (and displacement) of pieces, claiming of routes or occupation of the cities.  Each decision seem to lend itself to the bigger picture and I can't imagine anything being done simply because there isn't a better option otherwise.

However, there are two reservations I have which prevent me from rating it higher.  First is in the seemingly imbalance of the skills.  Actions alone feels way too powerful and as a result usually becomes the most hotly contested spot from the start.  Even though it has been argued that one can remain competitive with just 3 actions, 4 will allow you to claim almost every paths on the map in a single turn unopposed.  Thus, it is essential that no one is allowed to take the action upgrade unchallenged, tying up valuable resources for several people.

The other is in the weakness of the tokens, both in their usefulness and as a direct result of the randomness in which they are distributed on the board.  Therefore, they are largely ignored and seen as a poorly conceived notion.  Why even bother including it if they cannot be employed as a viable strategy to win?

Anyway, these are just impression from a single play, so they may prove to be incorrect later on.  Definitely interested in exploring Hansa Teutonica further, seeing that we had fibbed on a couple of rules as well.

Update #1: Finally got a 2nd chance at this and I've subsequently lowered my rating.  I still think it has a lot of interesting mechanics, but I'm leaning towards the opinion that overall the game is not quite balanced than I would have liked.  For example, if most of the players are interested in advancing their personal skill tracks rather than claiming cities, most of the board remains empty with activities centered around certain routes and the victory condition will be reached ever so slowly.

Yes, there are many ways to score points, but there is usually a high chance that the strategy employed will be lob sided rather than balanced.  Therefore, a portion of the board becomes underutilized and even neglected.  To me, I think that is an indication of poor design, if the game board itself isn't used to its full potential every single time.

RATING: 5
After 5+ Plays: I enjoyed it more at first, but after a few plays I began to avoid it.  I think it suffers a lot from a static set-up, so that each game seems to follow the same path.  The beginning of the game is particularly boring, because everyone must of necessity focus on getting additional actions if they hope to have any chance of winning.  

-----

The expansion may improve this game quite a bit.  Just having a new map makes the game good for a few more plays, but the expansion map also seems to make other strategies viable, which hopefully will lead to more long term variety in how the game unfolds.
